Refutation of Four Objections to the First Proof
Refuting four specific objections against the first rational proof for seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four objections against the first proof for the validity of seeing God are refuted using the concept of 'cause as suitable object'.

Core Idea
Four objections fall because the cause of vision is being a suitable object, not a privative or common attribute.

Sanusi now defends his first proof by addressing four specific objections. Each objection tries to undermine the proof by pointing to a supposed flaw in the causal link. The refutations rely on a precise understanding of what constitutes a cause for vision: being an existent suitable for sight, not any privative or common attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the flow of objections and responses.
The passage is dense; read slowly to catch each objection.
2
Identify the four objections
Look for the words 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع'. Each introduces an objection. Note the core claim of each.
The fourth objection includes its own refutation within the passage.
3
Understand the refutation for each
For each objection, find how Sanusi refutes it. The refutations are concise and rely on the nature of cause as 'what is suitable as an object of vision'.
Some refutations are explicit, others implicit from earlier reasoning.
4
Connect to the core idea
Notice how all objections misunderstand the cause of vision. The cause is not a privative attribute (first), nor possibility (second), nor does similarity require identical cause (third), nor does shared cause guarantee same ruling without conditions (fourth).
The key is that vision's cause is simply existence with suitability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينئذ يندفع اعتراضات الأول: أن صحة الرؤية أيضاً عدمية فلتكن علتهما كذلك. الثاني : أن من المشترك بينهما الإمكان فليكن هو العلة وذلك لأنه أيضاً عدمي ومشترك بين الموجود والمعدوم مع امتناع رؤيته . الثالث: أنه لو سلم تماثل الصحتين فالواحد النوعي قد يعلل بعلل مختلفة، وذلك لأن الرؤية قد تتعلق بالشيء من غير أن تدرك جوهريته أو عرضيته فضلاً عن زيادة كيف . وقد نرى زيداً إبان تتعلق رؤية واحدة بهويته ثم ربما نفصله إلى جواهر وأعراض وربما نغفل عن ذلك بحيث لا نعلمه ولو بعد التأمل . الرابع : أن مع الاشتراك في العلة قد لا يثبت الحكم لتفرد الأصل بشرط أو التفرع بمانع، وذلك لأن صحة الرؤية عند تحقق ما يصلح متعلقاً لها ضروري وأما منع اشتراك الوجود فمدفوع بما سبق ولزوم صحة رؤية كل موجود حتى الأصوات والطعوم والروائح والاعتقادات وغير ذلك ملتزم وانكارها استبعاد وعدم الرؤية متحقق كسائر العاديات
And then objections are refuted. The first: that the validity of vision is also privative, so let its cause be likewise. The second: that what is common between them is possibility (imkan), so let it be the cause, because it is also privative and common between existent and non-existent, while vision of non-existent is impossible. The third: that even if the two validities are similar, a single type may be caused by different causes, because vision may be attached to a thing without perceiving its substantiality or accidentally, let alone additional quality. We may see Zayd at the time a single vision attaches to his identity, then perhaps we distinguish him into substances and accidents, and perhaps we are unaware of that such that we do not know it even after reflection. The fourth: that despite the sharing of the cause, the ruling may not be established due to the original being unique with a condition or the branch having a preventer; because the validity of vision when what is suitable as its object is realized is necessary; as for denying the sharing of existence, it is refuted by what preceded, and the necessity of the validity of vision of every existent, even sounds, tastes, smells, beliefs, and other things, is admitted, and its denial is improbability, and the non-occurrence of vision is realized like other customary matters.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage lists and refutes four objections against the first rational proof. The objections are presented in sequence, and the refutations are implied or explicit based on the earlier definition of 'cause' as 'what is suitable as an object of vision'. The first objection claims that since the validity of vision is a privative (non-existential) attribute, its cause should also be privative (like temporal origination). The second objection suggests possibility (imkan) as the common cause, but it is privative and applies to non-existent things, which cannot be seen. The third objection argues that even if both substances and accidents share the same type of validity, a single type can have multiple causes; the answer is that vision does not require perception of essence or accident. The fourth objection notes that sharing a cause does not guarantee the same effect due to additional conditions or preventers; the answer is that when the suitable object is present, vision is necessary, and denying existence as the common cause is refuted. The passage accepts the consequence that all existents (including sounds, tastes, etc.) could in principle be seen, but this is not actually observed—it is a customary non-occurrence, not an impossibility.

Key Terms
صحة الرؤية
عدمية
الإمكان
الواحد النوعي
علل مختلفة
اشتراك العلة
العدادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cause of vision must be an efficient cause, like temporal origination or possibility.
Correction: The cause of vision is not an efficient cause but the condition of being a suitable object, i.e., existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objection (suggesting possibility as the common cause) fail?
A) Possibility is not shared between God and creatures
B) Possibility is privative and applies to non-existent things, which cannot be seen
C) Possibility is a type of existence
D) Possibility is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
The four objections fail because they misunderstand the nature of the cause of vision, which is simply being an existent suitable for sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the only condition for seeing something is that it exists, then why does your heart sometimes doubt that you will see Allah? What 'objections' have you internalized that make you feel distant from that vision?
?
When you read that non-occurrence of vision is merely customary, not impossible, do you truly believe that everything existent—including sounds and beliefs—could be seen by Allah’s power? Does your faith stretch that far, or do you still feel limits?
?
The refutation says that vision can attach to a thing without perceiving its essence. If you could see Allah without fully comprehending Him, would that satisfy you, or do you demand a complete comprehension that you know is impossible? What does that reveal about your heart's longing?

The Second Rational Proof and Its First Objection
Presentation of the second rational proof and refutation of its first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof for the possibility of seeing God, including why existence is identified as the cause for visibility and how the first objection (that possibility is conceptual) is refuted.

Core Idea
Vision requires existence; God exists—so He can be seen.

The early Sunni theologians formulated a rational proof for the possibility of seeing God based on our everyday experience of seeing substances and accidents. They argued that the validity of vision always corresponds to the existence of the seen object, implying a cause common to all visible things. This lesson unpacks that argument, examines an objection, and shows how the objection is resolved by clarifying what 'cause' means in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the entire passage once to grasp the overall flow of the argument: the proof, the objection, and the refutation.
The passage is dense; don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the core premise
Focus on why the validity of vision is tied to existence. Ask: What does the author mean by 'validity' (ṣiḥḥa) and how does it relate to existence?
3
Understand the exclusion of huduth
The proof argues that temporal origination (ḥudūth) cannot be the cause because it involves non-existence. Why is non-existence not a valid cause?
Non-existence is purely conceptual and cannot ground real phenomena.
4
Analyze the objection and refutation
The objection claims validity is just possibility (imkān), a conceptual matter. The refutation says conceptual things cannot be objects of vision. Compare this to the earlier part of the proof about existence.
Note Imam al-Haramayn's clarification that 'cause' means 'what is suitable as an object of vision.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : تمسك المتقدمون من أهل السنة في إمكان الرؤية بدليل عقلي . تقريره أنا نرى الجواهر والأعراض بحكم الضرورة كالأجسام والأضواء والألوان والأكوان، وباتفاق الخصوم. وإن زعم البعض منهم في بعض الأعراض أنها أجسام وفي الطول الذي هو جواهر ممتدة أنه عرضه ورد بأنه يدرك الطول بمجرد تأليف عدة من الجواهر في سمت وإن لم يخطر بالبال شيء من الأعراض. وقد يستدل على رؤية القبيلين بأنا نميز بالبصر بين نوع ونوع من الأجسام كالشجر والحجر، ونوع ونوع من الألوان كالسواد والبياض من غير أن يقوم شيء منها بآلة الإبصار. وبالجملة لما صحت رؤيتهما وصحة الرؤية أمر يتحقق عند الوجوب، وينتفي عند العدم لزم أن يكون لها علة لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح ، وأن يكون تلك العلة مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض لما مر من امتناع تعليل الواحد بعلتين وهي إما الوجود وإما الحدوث، إذ لا ثالث يصح للعلية والحدوث أيضاً غير صالح لأنه بالعدم وهو اعتباري محض أو عن الوجود بعد العدم. ولا مدخل للعدم فتعين الوجود، وهو مما يشترك فيه الواجب لما مر في مبحث الوجود عبارة عن مسبوقية الوجود فلزم صحة رؤيته وهو المطلوب . واعترض عليه بوجوه يندفع أكثرها بما دل عليه كلام إمام الحرمين من أن المراد بالعلة ها هنا ما يصلح متعلقاً بالرؤية لا المؤثر في الصحة على ما فهمه الأكثرون. فالاعتراض الأول : أن الصحة معناها الإمكان وهو أمر اعتباري لا يفتقر إلى علة موجودة بل يكفيه الحدوث الذي هو أيضاً اعتباري ووجه اندفاعه أن ما لا تحقق له في الأعيان لا يصلح متعلقاً للرؤية بالضرورة
And the second of them: the early people of Ahl al-Sunna adhered to a rational proof for the possibility of vision. Its exposition: We see substances and accidents by necessity, such as bodies, lights, colors, and [spatial] events, by agreement of the opponents. Even if some of them claim regarding some accidents that they are bodies, and regarding length—which is extended substances—that it is an accident, it is refuted because length is perceived merely by arranging several substances in a line, even if no accident occurs to the mind. One may also argue for the visibility of both types by the fact that we distinguish by sight between types of bodies (e.g., tree and stone) and between types of colors (e.g., black and white), without anything from them subsisting in the organ of sight. In summary, since their vision is valid, and the validity of vision is realized when [the thing] exists and is negated when it is non-existent, it must have a cause—because there is no preponderance without a preponderator—and that cause must be common between substance and accident, as passed [in the argument] about the impossibility of a single thing being caused by two causes. It is either existence or temporal origination, since there is no third valid for causality. Temporal origination is also not valid because it is [composed] of non-existence, which is purely conceptual, or of existence after non-existence. Non-existence has no entry [into causality], so existence is determined, and it is something that the Necessary shares in, as passed in the discussion of existence being expression of being preceded by non-existence? So the validity of seeing Him follows, and that is the desired conclusion. And it was objected to with [several] aspects, most of which are refuted by what the statement of Imam al-Haramayn indicates: that what is meant by 'cause' here is what is suitable as an object of vision, not the efficient cause of validity, as most understood. So the first objection: that 'validity' means possibility (imkan), and it is a conceptual matter not needing an existent cause; rather, temporal origination (huduth) suffices, which is also conceptual. And the aspect of its refutation: that which has no realization in external entities is not suitable as an object of vision by necessity.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational proof for the possibility of seeing God, attributed to the early Ahl al-Sunna. The proof argues that we see substances and accidents by necessity, and the validity of vision depends on the existence of the seen object. Therefore, there must be a common cause for the validity of vision, which is existence, because temporal origination is not valid. Since God shares existence, He can be seen. The passage then introduces objections, but states most are refuted by Imam al-Haramayn's clarification that 'cause' means 'what is suitable as an object of vision,' not an efficient cause. The first objection claims that validity is just possibility (imkan), which is conceptual and does not need an existent cause; temporal origination would suffice. The refutation is that something conceptual cannot be a real object of vision—only things with external existence are suitable. The passage ends with that refutation.

Key Terms
العِلَّة
الإمكان
الحدوث
الوجود
ما يصلح متعلقاً للرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'cause of vision' refers to an efficient cause that produces vision.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn clarified that 'cause' here means 'what is suitable as an object of vision' (mā yaṣluḥu muta‘allaqan lil-ru’ya), not an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is temporal origination (al-huduth) not a valid cause for the validity of vision?
A) Because it is not shared by God
B) Because it is composed of non-existence, which is purely conceptual
C) Because it is not a cause at all
D) Because it is a type of existence

Key Takeaway
The second rational proof demonstrates that existence is the condition for visibility, so God, who exists necessarily, can be seen; the objection that possibility is merely conceptual is refuted because vision requires a real existent object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot see God now, yet you are certain that you will see Him in the afterlife, does that certainty shape your daily decisions? What is one decision today that would be different if you lived with the constant awareness that you will see Him?
?
The proof shows that existence is the condition for visibility. Do you live as if God's existence is the most real thing in your life—more real than your emotions, your problems, your plans? Or has His existence become a conceptual abstraction? What would it take to make His existence more immediate to you?
?
The objection treated possibility as merely conceptual. Do you treat your own faith as a conceptual belief or as a living reality that demands action? If you truly believed that the One who sees you now will be seen by you personally, what would you change about how you use your eyes today?

Fourth Objection: Particularity as Condition or Obstacle
Sanusi states the fourth objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that essence-specific conditions could block the vision of God by distinguishing between validity and actual occurrence of vision.

Core Idea
Conditions affect actual occurrence, not possibility.

Sanusi presents the fourth objection to the commonality of existence as the basis for vision. The objector argues that even if existence is common, the particularities of substance, accident, or necessity might condition or obstruct vision. The author's reply hinges on a crucial distinction: between whether vision is valid (sihha) and whether it actually happens. This passage sharpens our understanding of the kalam method of separating metaphysical possibility from contingent actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector concedes that existence is the common cause but raises the possibility of conditions specific to essence.
The objection is based on a distinction between common cause and specific conditions.
2
Identify the key distinction in the reply
Locate where the author distinguishes between sihha (validity) and tahaqquq (actual occurrence). Understanding this difference is central to the refutation.
The word 'sihha' is used twice; note the textual variant mentioned in the footnote.
3
Understand the implication for the vision of God
Reflect on why this distinction answers the objection. If conditions only affect actual occurrence, not validity, then the possibility of seeing God remains intact.
This shows the importance of precise terminology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن بعد ثبوت كون الوجود هو العلة، وكونه مشتركاً بين الجوهر والعرض وبين الواجب لا يلزم من صحة رؤيتهما صحة رؤيته لجواز أن تكون خصوصية الجوهرية أو العرضية شرطاً لها أو خصوصية الواجبية مانعاً عنها. ووجه اندفاعه أن صحة رؤيته الشيء الذي له الوجود الذي هو المتعلق للرؤية ضرورية بل لا معنى لصحة رؤيته إلا ذلك، ثم الشرطية أو المانعية إنما يتصور لتحقق الرؤية لا لصحتها، وقد يعترض بوجوه أخر. (۱) سقط من (ب) لفظ (صحة).
The fourth [objection]: that after it is established that existence is the cause, and that it is common between substance and accident and between the Necessary, it does not follow from the validity of seeing them [substance and accident] the validity of seeing Him [God], because it is possible that the particularity of substance-ness or accident-ness be a condition for it, or the particularity of necessity be an obstacle against it. And the repelling of it is that the validity of seeing a thing that has existence—which is the object of vision—is necessary; indeed, there is no meaning to the validity of seeing it except that. Then conditionality (shartiyya) or obstacle-ness (mani'iyya) is conceivable for the actual occurrence (tahaqquq) of vision, not for its validity (sihha). And there are other objections. (1) In (b) the word 'sihha' is omitted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection. The objector concedes that existence is the common cause of visibility for substances and accidents, but argues that the particularity of being substance or accident might be a necessary condition for visibility, while the particularity of being necessary (wajib) might be an obstacle. The answer distinguishes between the validity of vision (sihhat al-ru'ya) and its actual occurrence (tahaqquq al-ru'ya). The condition or obstacle affects whether the vision actually happens, not whether it is possible. The validity of seeing something requires only that it has existence. The footnote indicates a textual variant where the word 'sihha' is missing in one manuscript.

Key Terms
الشرطية
المانعية
تحقق الرؤية
صحة الرؤية
الخصوصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a condition is required for vision, then vision is impossible.
Correction: The author clarifies that conditions only affect actual occurrence (tahaqquq), not validity (sihha). So a condition does not preclude the possibility of vision.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fourth objection, what does a condition affect?
A) The validity of vision.
B) The actual occurrence of vision.
C) The existence of the object.
D) The commonality of existence.

Key Takeaway
The validity of seeing something depends only on its existence; conditions affect whether the vision actually occurs, not whether it is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your own particular sins or shortcomings make it impossible for you to draw near to God or to hope for the vision of Him in the afterlife? This lesson teaches that those particularities only affect actual occurrence, not the validity of vision. How does this change the way you approach God now? Will you begin to pray with more confidence in His mercy?
?
The objection raised the possibility that God's necessity (wujub) might be an obstacle to seeing Him. Do you ever treat God's majesty or transcendence as a wall that keeps you distant? The refutation says that only conditions for actual occurrence, not validity, are affected. Will you now actively seek closeness to Him, knowing that the validity of seeing Him is already established by His existence?

Objection: Existence Is Not Shared
Denies that existence is common between the Necessary and contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence is not shared between the Necessary and contingent beings, and how redefining existence as 'having identity' resolves it.

Core Idea
Existence as 'having identity' allows it to be common to all existents.

This lesson presents a key objection to the Ash'ari position on the commonality of existence. The objection claims that if each thing's existence is identical to its essence, then existence cannot be shared between God and creatures. We'll see how the author resolves this by redefining existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Read the objection carefully: it denies that existence is common, because each thing's existence is identical to its essence, and essences differ.
The objector equates existence with essence.
2
Understand the answer's reference
The answer refers back to an earlier discussion on existence. Note that it targets the literal Ash'ari position.
The answer implies that the objection only holds if we take the Ash'ari statement at face value.
3
Grasp the redefinition of existence
The answer redefines existence as 'a thing having identity' (kawn al-shay' lahu huwiyya). This is the key move that makes sharing possible.
Identity (huwiyya) is a common concept.
4
See how sharing becomes necessary
Once existence is defined as having identity, then any existent necessarily shares in 'having identity', so existence is common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: منع اشتراك الوجود بين الواجب وغيره بل وجود كل شيء عين حقيقته، ولا خفاء في أن حقيقة الواجب لا تماثل حقيقة الممكن، وحقيقة الإنسان لا تماثل حقيقة الفرس. وجوابه على ما (۱) مرَّ في بحث الوجود غاية الأمر أن الاعتراض يرد على الأشعري التزاماً ما دام كلامه محمولاً على ظاهره. وأما بعد تحقيق أن الوجود هو كون الشيء له هوية فاشتراكه ضروري . (۱) في (ب) بزيادة لفظ (على).
The first [additional objection]: denial that existence is common between the Necessary and other than Him; rather the existence of each thing is identical to its essence (dhāt), and there is no concealment that the essence of the Necessary is not similar to the essence of the possible, and the essence of man is not similar to the essence of the horse. And its answer: based on what passed in the discussion of existence, the matter is that the objection is directed against the Ash'ari as a commitment as long as his statement is interpreted literally (dhāhir). But after the verification that existence is a thing having identity (huwiyya), then its sharing is necessary. (1) In (b) an addition of the word 'على'.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the first additional objection and its answer. The objector denies that existence is common between the Necessary (God) and contingent beings; rather, each thing's existence is identical to its essence (dhāt). Since essences differ, existence differs. The answer refers back to the earlier discussion on existence: if one takes the Ash'ari position literally, this objection applies. However, after analysis, existence is defined as 'a thing having identity' (kawn al-shay' lahu huwiyya), which is shared necessarily by all existents. The footnote notes a textual variant where 'على' is added in one manuscript.

Correction: The author redefines existence as 'having identity', which is common to all existents, so sharing is necessary.

Checkpoint

A) Existence is a mental concept.
B) Existence is identical to essence.
C) Existence is a thing having identity.
D) Existence is a privation.

Key Takeaway
Existence is common because it means 'having identity', not being identical to essence.

How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed that your very identity is a gift from the Necessary being? Commit to a daily dhikr that grounds you in this reality.
?

Imam al-Razi on the Object of Vision
Imam al-Razi quotes and critiques a rival Ash'ari view on the object of vision, then gives his own position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's position that existence is the basis for visibility, but we see specific essences, not bare existence.

Core Idea
Existence enables visibility, but we see real distinct things.

Imam al-Razi engages with an extreme Ash'ari view that reduces all vision to seeing mere existence. His response defends both the role of existence in making visibility possible and the ordinary experience of seeing distinct objects. This passage clarifies a nuanced position in kalam on divine and human vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a reported view, a criticism, and al-Razi's own position.
The key term 'mukābara' indicates strong disapproval.
2
Identify the rejected view
The view al-Razi rejects is that the visible is only existence and that differences are known only by necessity, not sight. Identify why this might be considered problematic.
3
Understand al-Razi's alternative
Al-Razi states that existence is a cause (ʿilla) for the validity of a specific essence being visible. Reflect on how this preserves both the role of existence and the perception of distinctions.
This is a middle ground: existence is necessary but not sufficient for vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الرازي في نهاية العقول من أصحابنا من التزم أن المرئي هو الوجود فقط، وأنا لا نبصر اختلاف المختلفات بل نعلمه بالضرورة، وهذا مكابرة لا نرتضيها، بل الوجود علة لصحة كون الحقيقة المخصوصة مرئية.
And Imam al-Razi said in Nihayat al-Uqool: 'Among our companions is one who committed to [the view] that the visible (al-mar'ī) is only existence, and that we do not see the differences of distinct things, but rather know it necessarily. This is a stubborn denial (mukābara) that we do not approve. Rather, existence is a cause for the validity of a specific essence being visible.'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage quotes Imam al-Razi (d. 606/1209) from his work Nihayat al-Uqool. Al-Razi reports that some Ash'ari scholars held that what is seen is only existence itself, and that we do not visually perceive the differences between things (like color, shape) but only know them by necessity. Al-Razi calls this a 'stubborn denial' (mukābara) and rejects it. His own view is that existence is the cause of the validity of visibility, but it enables specific essences (e.g., Zayd, black) to be seen. In other words, existence is the basis for visibility, but we see real things with their distinctions.

Correction: Al-Razi explicitly affirms that existence is a cause (ʿilla) for the validity of a specific essence being visible.

Checkpoint

A) Existence is the only thing we see.
B) Existence is the cause for a specific essence to be visible.
C) Existence is irrelevant to visibility.
D) We see essences without existence.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi holds that existence makes visibility possible, but we see specific essences, not bare existence.

Refuting the Universal Visibility Objection
The author refutes the objection that ru'ya would necessitate seeing all existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that ru'ya would apply to all existents is refuted through the concept of God's customary action (jary al-'ādah).

Core Idea
The fact that we don't see sounds doesn't prove seeing God is impossible.

The second objection against the possibility of seeing God argues that if existence were the basis, then every existent should be visible, which is absurd. This objection confuses impossibility with what God simply does not do as a matter of custom. In this passage, the author provides a clear refutation by distinguishing between what is impossible and what is merely not customary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the objector's claim that if existence suffices for visibility, then all existents must be visible, including sounds, tastes, etc., which is obviously false.
The objection uses reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the answer
The author denies the falsehood of the conclusion and explains that the non-visibility of some existents is due to God's customary action (jary al-‘ādah) of not creating sight in us for them, not because it is intrinsically impossible.
This shifts the discussion from necessity to custom.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the difference between impossibility (imtina‘) and improbability (istib‘ād). The objection is dismissed as mere improbability, which is not a valid logical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه يلزم على ما ذكرتم صحة رؤية كل موجود حتى الأصوات والطعوم والروايح والاعتقادات ، والقدر والإرادات وأنواع الإدراكات وغير ذلك من الموجودات وبطلانه ضروري. والجواب : منع بطلانه وأن ما لا يتعلق بها الرؤية بناء على جري العادة بأن الله تعالى لا يخلق فينا رؤيتها لا بناء على امتناع ذلك ، وما ذكره الخصم مجرد استبعاد .
Second: That according to what you mentioned, it would necessitate the validity of seeing every existent, even sounds, tastes, smells, beliefs, capacities, wills, types of perceptions, and other existents; and its falsehood is necessary. The answer: We deny its falsehood and that what is not seen is based on the customary way (jary al-‘ādah) that God Most High does not create in us the sight of it, not based on the impossibility of that. What the opponent mentioned is mere improbability.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the proof that ru'ya (seeing God) is possible based on existence. The objector argues: if visibility depends solely on existence, then every existent must be visible, including sounds, tastes, etc., which is clearly false. The author responds by denying the conclusion's falsehood: the fact that some existents are not seen is due to God's customary action (jary al-‘ādah) of not creating sight in us for them, not because it is intrinsically impossible. The opponent's claim is merely an improbability (istib‘ād), not a logical refutation.

Key Terms

موجود
جري العادة
استبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is existent and existents can be seen, then all existents must be visible.
Correction: Visibility is not a necessary consequence of existence; it depends on God's creative act. Some existents are not seen because God does not create sight in us for them as a matter of custom, not because it is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Because God does not create sight in us for them by custom.
C) Because they are not accidental.
D) Because they are not visible by nature.

Key Takeaway
The objection that ru'ya would require all existents to be seen is refuted by appealing to divine custom, not impossibility.

Answering the Objection from Createdness
Refutation of the objection that createdness undermines the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that existence would imply God's createdness by distinguishing between conceptual attributes and real efficient causes.

Core Idea
Not every shared attribute requires a real cause; some are merely conceptual.

This lesson addresses a sophisticated objection to the proof that existence is the cause of the vision of God. The objector argues that if existence is the common cause for substance and accident sharing createdness, then God would also be created. The author responds by distinguishing between conceptual and real attributes, showing that createdness is a conceptual matter that does not require a cause like existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The objector claims that the proof for existence as a cause is undermined because createdness is shared by substances and accidents, and the only common factor is existence, so God would be created.
Notice the logical structure: shared property → common cause → undesirable consequence.
2
Identify the author's distinction
The author answers that createdness is a purely conceptual matter (amr i'tibari mahd) that does not necessitate an external cause. Understand what 'conceptual' means here—it is not a real attribute that depends on existence.
Contrast with properties like 'visibility' that require real existence.
3
Examine the concession about temporality
The author concedes but clarifies that temporal origination (huduth) is the actual cause of createdness, not existence itself. This shows that the objector's assumed cause (existence) is not the correct one.
This concession actually strengthens the distinction.
4
Reflect on the final note
The author notes that the objection from tangibility is strong, showing the weakness of the original proof. But the proper understanding is that the cause is the 'object of vision,' which is existence itself.
This nuance saves the original proof from being entirely abandoned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن نقض الدليل بصحة المخلوقية، فإنها مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض ولا مشترك بينهما يصلح علة لذلك سوى الوجود فيلزم صحة مخلوقية الواجب وهو محال . والجواب : أنها أمر اعتباري محض لا يقتضي علة. إذ ليست مما يتحقق عنـد الوجود وينتفي عند المعدم كصحة الرؤية. سلمنا لكن الحدوث يصلح ها هنا علة . | لأن المانع من ذلك في صحة الرؤية ، إنما هو امتناع تعلق الرؤية بما لا تحقق له في الخارج، وأما النقض بصحة الملموسة فقوي والإنصاف أن ضعف هذا الدليل جلي، وعلى ما ذكرنا من أن المراد بالعلة ها هنا متعلق الرؤية يكون المرئي من (۳) كل شيء وجوده.
Third: That the proof is undermined by the validity of createdness (al-makhlūqiyya), for it is shared between substance and accident, and there is nothing shared between them suitable as a cause for that except existence, so the validity of createdness of the Necessary Being would follow, which is impossible. The answer: It (createdness) is a purely conceptual matter (amr i‘tibārī maḥḍ) that does not necessitate a cause. For it is not of the things that are realized upon existence and vanish upon non-existence like the validity of vision (ru’ya). We concede, but temporality (al-ḥudūth) is a suitable cause here. Because the impediment to that in the validity of vision is only the impossibility of vision attaching to what has no realization in externality. As for the objection by the validity of being tangible (al-malmūsa), it is strong, and honestly the weakness of this proof is clear. And according to what we mentioned that what is meant by the cause here is the object of vision, the visible of every thing is its existence.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objector argues: the proof (that existence is the cause of ru'ya) is undermined by the fact that createdness (makhluqiyya) is also common to all existents, so if existence is the cause, then God would be created, which is false. The author answers that createdness is a purely conceptual attribute (amr i‘tibārī) that does not require a real cause like ru'ya does. He further concedes but clarifies that temporal origination (ḥudūth) is the actual cause of createdness, not existence. He also notes that the objection from the tangibility of things is strong, showing the weakness of the original proof, but the proper understanding is that the cause is the 'object of vision' (muta‘alliq al-ru’ya), which is existence itself.

أمر اعتباري
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence alone is the cause of all common properties, so God would be created if existence is the cause of any shared attribute.
Correction: The author shows that not all common properties are real attributes; some are merely conceptual and do not require an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that existence would make God created?
A) By denying that existence is shared.
B) By saying createdness is a conceptual matter not needing a cause.
C) By claiming God is not existent.
D) By accepting the objection as valid.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between conceptual and real attributes is crucial for avoiding false conclusions about God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might this error lead you to misunderstand God's transcendence? Will you commit to examining your mental frameworks and submitting them to what revelation actually teaches?
?

?
If conceptual attributes do not necessitate a cause, then what does that say about your own mental constructs about God? Are you willing to let go of concepts that are merely human frameworks and fully trust in God's revealed description of Himself, even when it challenges your assumptions?

Existence as the Cause of Visibility
Al-Razi rejects the view that only existence is seen and affirms existence as the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that existence is the cause that makes a specific essence visible, and why he rejects the opposing view that only existence is seen.

Core Idea
Existence enables visibility of specific essences, it is not the sole content of vision.

Imam al-Razi engages in an intra-school debate about what exactly is seen when we see something. Some Ash‘arites claimed that only existence itself is seen, and that differences between things are known by necessity rather than sight. Razi dismisses this as a stubborn denial and offers a more nuanced position: existence is the cause that allows a specific essence to be visible, but the vision is of that essence, not just existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get the gist of Razi's argument. Pay attention to the key terms.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Look for the view Razi rejects (that only existence is seen and differences are known by necessity) and the view he affirms (existence is the cause for a specific essence being visible).
Note that Razi calls the rejected view 'mukābarah' (stubborn denial).
3
Understand the logical relationship
Reflect on why Razi insists that existence is the cause (ʿilla) rather than the object of vision. Ask yourself: How does this preserve the possibility of seeing distinct things while grounding visibility in existence?
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الرازي في نهاية العقول من أصحابنا من التزم أن المرئي هو الوجود فقط، وأنا لا نبصر اختلاف المختلفات بل نعلمه بالضرورة، وهذا مكابرة لا نرتضيها، بل الوجود علة لصحة كون الحقيقة المخصوصة مرئية .
Imam al-Razi said in Nihāyat al-‘Uqūl: Among our companions there is one who insisted that the visible (al-mar’ī) is only existence, and that we do not see the differences between distinct things but know them necessarily. This is a stubborn denial (mukābarah) that we do not approve. Rather, existence is the cause (illa) for the validity of a specific essence (al-ḥaqīqah al-makhṣūṣah) being visible.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage quotes Imam al-Razi's view on the 'illa (cause) of ru'ya. Al-Razi criticizes a group among his own school (Ash‘arites) who insisted that what is seen is only existence (al-wujūd) itself, not specific essences, and that we do not see differences between things but know them by necessity. Al-Razi calls this a 'stubborn denial' (mukābarah) and rejects it. Instead, he affirms that existence is the cause (ʿilla) for the validity of a specific essence being visible—meaning existence makes it possible for a particular entity (like God) to be seen, but the vision itself is of that specific essence, not of bare existence.

Key Terms
المرئي
الوجود
علة
الحقيقة المخصوصة
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The visible is only existence itself, and differences among things are known by necessity, not sight.
Correction: Razi holds that existence is the cause that makes a specific essence visible, but the act of seeing is of that specific essence, not of bare existence.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what is the relationship between existence and visibility?
A) Existence is the sole content of vision.
B) Existence makes it possible for a specific essence to be visible.
C) Existence has no relation to visibility.
D) Existence is an obstacle to visibility.

Key Takeaway
Existence is the enabling cause for a specific essence to be visible, not the sole object of vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you ever think you are seeing pure 'existence' rather than distinct things? How does this lesson challenge your unexamined assumptions about perception?
?
Al-Razi calls the opposing view 'mukabarah'—a stubborn denial. Is there an area of your faith where you hold a view stubbornly despite clear evidence? What would it take for you to admit you might be wrong?
?
If existence is the cause enabling visibility, then everything you see is a sign of existence itself. How should this change the way you look at the ordinary things around you today? What will you do differently?

The Quranic Proof for Ru'ya and the Meaning of Nazar
Sanusi cites the Quranic proof for ru'ya and defends the interpretation of nazar as vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Wujuhun yawma'idhin nadirah ila rabbiha nazirah' serves as proof for seeing God in the afterlife, and how the word 'nazar' is interpreted by the Salaf.

Core Idea
The verse about 'faces looking at their Lord' is the strongest Quranic proof for ru'ya, with 'nazar' meaning vision.

Sanusi begins by asserting the consensus on the occurrence of ru'ya before dissenters appeared. He then presents the key Quranic verse from Surah al-Qiyamah, explaining that the word nazar connected to 'ila' must mean vision, its necessary concomitant, or a metaphor confirmed by reason and custom. An objection that nazar could mean 'waiting' is raised and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the flow from consensus to verse to objection and answer.
Pay attention to the three interpretations listed.
2
Identify the three possible meanings of nazar
Find the three interpretations Sanusi gives for 'nazar' connected to 'ila': literal vision, its necessary concomitant, or a metaphor. Notice how each is justified.
These are the key positions he defends.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says nazar could mean 'waiting' (intizar). List the supporting arguments (poetic evidence, ila as noun, etc.). Then see how Sanusi refutes it using context, Salaf practice, and the principle of not deviating from the literal without a clue.
The answer emphasizes the importance of context and Salaf interpretation.
4
Reflect on the Salaf's role
Notice that Sanusi appeals to the fact that the Salaf did not deviate from treating 'ila' as a preposition. Reflect on why the Salaf's understanding is authoritative in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة وقوع الرؤية بالنص والإجماع (وعلى الوقوع إجماع الأمة قبل حدوث المخالف والنص فمن الكتاب، قوله تعالى {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة} (القيامة:22-23) والنظر الموصول بإلى إما بمعنى الرؤية، أو ملزوم لها، أو مجاز تتعين فيها شهادة العقل والاستعمال ،والعرف واعترض بأنه قد يكون بمعنى الانتظار كقول الشاعر: وجوه ناظرات يوم بدر إلى الرحمن تأتي بالفلاح (۱) وإلى قد تكون اسماً بمعنى النعمة، والنظر قد يتصف بما لا تتصف به الرؤية، كالشدة والازورار ونحوهما، وقد يوجد بدونها مثل نظرت إلى الهلال فلم أره، وتقديره إلى ثواب ربها احتمال ظاهر منقول. وأجيب بأن الانتظار لا يلائم سوق الآية، ولا يليق بدار الثواب وكون إلى ها هنا حرفاً ظاهر لم يعدل عنه السلف. وجعل النظر الموصول بإلى للانتظار تعسف، وكذا العدول عن الحقيقة أو المجاز المشهور إلى الحدث (۳) بلا قرينة تعين، ومنه قوله تعالى في تعيير الكفار وتحقيرهم {كلا إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ المحجوبون} (المطففين:15) وقوله تعالى {للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة} (يونس:26) أي الرؤية بدلالة الخبر وشهادة السلف . ومن السنة قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إنكم سترون ربكم كما ترون هذا القمر ليلة البدر لا تضامون في رؤيته».
Proofs for the occurrence of vision (ru’ya) by text and consensus. (And upon its occurrence is the consensus of the community before the appearance of the dissenter, and the text from the Book is His saying Almighty: {Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord} (Q. 75:22-23). The word naẓar connected by ilā either means vision, or is a necessary concomitant of it, or a metaphor in which the testimony of reason, usage, and custom determines. And it was objected that it may mean waiting, as the poet said: “Faces looking (nāẓirāt) on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful, bringing success.” And ilā may be a noun meaning favor, and naẓar may be described with what vision is not described, such as intensity and squinting and the like, and may occur without it, like “I looked at the crescent but did not see it.” And its interpretation as “to the reward of their Lord” is a plausible transmitted possibility. And it was answered that waiting does not fit the context of the verse, nor is it suitable to the abode of reward. And that ilā here is a preposition, manifest, from which the Salaf did not deviate. And making naẓar connected by ilā to mean waiting is far-fetched, as is turning away from the literal meaning or the well-known metaphor to the figurative without a specifying clue. And from it is His saying Almighty in rebuking and belittling the disbelievers: {No indeed! On that day they will be veiled from their Lord} (Q. 83:15), and His saying Almighty: {For those who do good is the best and more} (Q. 10:26), i.e., the vision, as indicated by the report and the testimony of the Salaf. And from the Sunnah is his saying, peace be upon him: “You will see your Lord as you see this moon on the night of the full moon; you will not be crowded in seeing Him.”
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the textual evidence for the occurrence of ru'ya (seeing God) in the afterlife. It begins by invoking ijma‘ (consensus) before the emergence of dissenters and then cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Qiyamah (75:22-23). The author argues that the word naẓar (looking) connected by ilā (to) has three possible interpretations: literal vision, its necessary concomitant, or a metaphor determined by reason, usage, and custom. An objection is then raised: naẓar could mean ‘waiting’ (intizār), supported by a poetic example, and ilā could be a noun meaning ‘favor’. Also, naẓar can be described with attributes not applicable to vision (e.g., intensity, squinting) and can occur without actual seeing. The author answers this objection by pointing out that the waiting interpretation does not fit the context (sīyāq) of the verse, which is about the abode of reward, and that the Salaf consistently treated ilā as a preposition. Calling it waiting is far-fetched, and abandoning the literal meaning or common metaphor without a clear clue is unjustified. The author then provides two additional supporting texts: the veil verse (Q. 83:15), which implies that believers are not veiled, and the ‘more’ (ziyādah) in Q. 10:26, interpreted by the Salaf as ru'ya. Finally, a prophetic hadith compares seeing God to seeing the full moon without crowding.

Key Terms
رؤية
نظر
إلى
انتظار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the word 'nazar' in the verse simply means 'waiting', so the verse does not prove ru'ya.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'waiting' does not fit the context, and the Salaf interpreted 'nazar' as vision or its concomitant, supported by reason and custom.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the primary Quranic proof for the occurrence of ru'ya?
A) Surah al-Mutaffifin, verse 15
B) Surah al-Qiyamah, verses 22-23
C) Surah Yunus, verse 26
D) The hadith about seeing the moon

Key Takeaway
The verse from al-Qiyamah provides clear textual proof for ru'ya when understood correctly by the Salaf, and the objection that nazar means waiting is refuted by context and scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your face will be radiant on that Day if you are among those who see Allah. But is your face radiant now with the light of faith and hope? What is one thing you can change today to make your heart more longing for that vision?
?
When you encounter a verse that challenges your preconceptions, do you submit to the understanding of the Salaf, or do you rationalize to fit your own comfort? Are you willing to abandon your own reasoning for the sake of truth?
?
The objection to ru'ya ultimately stems from a reluctance to believe Allah would honor believers so greatly. Examine your heart: Do you truly believe that Allah's mercy is vast enough to grant you the vision of His face? What keeps you from full certainty in His promise?

Additional Proofs for Ru'ya from Quran and Sunnah
Sanusi provides supplementary proofs for ru'ya from the Quran and Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how other Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith support the occurrence of seeing God in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The veil verse implies believers see God, and 'ziyadah' in Surah Yunus refers to ru'ya, confirmed by the hadith.

After establishing the primary proof, Sanusi reinforces it with additional scriptural evidence. The verse about the disbelievers being veiled implies believers are not veiled, and the 'more' for the righteous is interpreted as ru'ya by the Salaf. Finally, a well-known hadith compares seeing God to seeing the full moon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it contains three distinct proofs: the veil verse, the ziyadah verse, and the hadith.
2
Understand the veil verse as proof
Consider how the verse 'No indeed! On that day they will be veiled from their Lord' (83:15) implies that believers are not veiled, thus they see Him.
This is an indirect proof.
3
Analyze the ziyadah verse
The verse 'For those who do good is the best and more' (10:26) is interpreted by the Salaf as 'the best' being Paradise and 'more' being the vision of God. See how Sanusi mentions this as a proof.
The interpretation relies on the report and Salaf testimony.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the prophetic hadith comparing seeing God to seeing the full moon. Note the phrase 'you will not be crowded' indicating clarity and ease.
This hadith is a direct and clear proof from Sunnah.

The Sunni Proof for Seeing Allah (Ru'ya) from Consensus and the Quran
Al-Taftazani presents the Sunni proof for ru'ya from consensus and the Quranic verse with linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni scriptural proof for ru'ya (seeing Allah in the afterlife) based on the consensus of the early community and the linguistic analysis of the Quranic verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is proven by scripture alone, and the Quranic phrase 'nazar ila' necessarily implies ru'ya.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating that the occurrence of ru'ya can only be established through scriptural evidence (sam'iyyat), not reason. He then presents two scriptural proofs: the consensus (ijma') of the early Muslim community and the Quranic text itself. The key verse is 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The linguistic analysis of 'nazar' connected with 'ila' leads to the conclusion that it means 'seeing' or its necessary concomitant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessity of scriptural proof
The author asserts that ru'ya cannot be proven rationally; only revelation can establish it. Recognize that this is a foundational principle in Sunni kalam.
This contrasts with Mu'tazilite rationalism.
2
Examine the consensus proof
The early community agreed on ru'ya before dissenters appeared, and the hadiths on ru'ya were transmitted by many companions. Note that ijma' here is a transmitted proof.
Twenty-one companions narrated the hadith of ru'ya.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse linguistically
The phrase 'ناظرة إلى ربها' is the central text. The word 'nazar' with 'ila' must mean either seeing or its necessary concomitant because the literal meaning (turning the pupil) is impossible for Allah.
This is a key argument against literal interpretation.
4
Consider the figurative exclusivity interpretation
The placement of 'nadhirah' before 'ila rabbiha' is for emphasis or to indicate that the believers are so absorbed in beholding Allah that they see nothing else.
This reinforces the meaning of seeing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم فيرفع الحجاب فينظرون إلى وجه الله تعالى(۱) عز وجل» وقوله : وأكرمهم على الله من ينظر إلى وجهه)) (٢) . قوله : وعلى الوقوع الإجماع والنص. أما الإجماع (۳) لا خفاء في أن إثبات وقوع الرؤية لا يمكن إلا بالأدلة السمعية. وقد احتجوا عليه بالإجماع والنص. أما الإجماع فاتفاق الأمة قبل حدوث المخالف على وقوع الرؤية. وكون الآيات والأحاديث الواردة فيها على ظواهرها حتى روى حديث الرؤية أحد وعشرون رجلاً من كبار الصحابة، وأما النص فمن الكتاب قوله تعالى وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة ) (4) فإن النظر الموصول بإلى إما بمعنى الرؤية، أو ملزوم لها بشهادة النقل عن أئمة اللغة، والتتبع الموارد استعماله وإما مجاز عنها لكونه عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة نحو المرئي طلباً لرؤيته وقد تعذر ههنا الحقيقة لامتناع المقابلة والجهة فتعين الرؤية لكونها أقرب المجازات بحيث التحق بالحقائق بشهادة العرف والتقديم بمجرد الاهتمام ورعاية الفاصلة دون الحصر أو للحصر ادعاء بمعنى أن المؤمنين لاستغراقهم في مشاهدة جماله ، وقصر النظر على عظمة جلاله، كأنهم لا يلتفتون إلى ما سواه ولا يرون إلا الله . واعترض بأن (إلى) ها هنا ليست حرفاً بل اسماً بمعنى النعمة واحد الآلاء وناظرة من النظر بمعنى الانتظار كما في قوله تعالى انظرونا نقتبس من نوركم) () ولو سلم فالموصول (بإلى) أيضاً قد يجيء بمعنى (1) الانتظار كما في قول الشاعر: وجوه ناظرات یوم بدر إلى الرحمن يأتي الفلاح وشعث ينظرون إلى بلال كما نظر الظماء حيا الغمام كل الخلائق ينظرون سجاله نظر الحجيج إلى طلوع هلال . ولو سلَّم ، فالنظر الموصول (بإلى) ليس بمعنى الرؤية، ولا ملزوماً لها (١) لاتصافه بما لم تتصف به الرؤية مثل الشدة والازورار والرضى والتحيز والذل والخشوع انتفاء الرؤية مثل : نظرت إلى الهلال فلم أره . ولتحققه مع قال الله تعالى وتراهم ينظرون إليك وهم لا يبصرون ) (۲) وجعله مجازاً عن الرؤية ليس بأولى من حمله على حذف المضاف أي ناظرة إلى ثواب ربها على ما ذكره علي رضي الله عنه، وكثير من المفسرين . وبالجملة : فلا خفاء في أن ما ذكرنا احتمالات تدفع الاحتجاج بالآية. وأجيب بأن سوق الآية لبشارة المؤمنين وبيان أنهم يومئذ في غاية الفرح والسرور، والإخبار بانتظارهم النعمة والثواب لا يلائم ذلك، بل ربما ينافيه، لأن من مع الانتظار موت أحمر، فهو بالغم والحزن والقلق وضيق الصدر أجدر، وإن كان القطع بالحصول على أن كون (إلى) اسماً بمعنى النعمة لو ثبت في اللغة فلا خفاء في بعده وغرابته وإخلاله بالفهم عند تعلق النظر به ولهذا لم يحمل الآية عليه (٤) أحد أئمة التفسير في القرن الأول والثاني بل أجمعوا على خلافه : وكون النظر الموصول (بإلى) سيما المستند إلى الوجه بمعنى الانتظار مما لم يثبت عن الثقات ولم تدل عليه الآيات لجواز أن يحمل على تقليب الحدقة بتأويلات لا تخفى. وأما اعتبار حذف المضاف فعدول عن الحقيقة أو المجاز المشهور في (٥) الحذف الذي لا يظهر فيه قرينة تعيين المحذوف، وتمام الكلام في الإشكالات الموردة من قبل المعتزلة على الاحتجاج بالآية والتقصي عنها من قبل أهل الحق مذكور في نهاية العقول (٦) للإمام الرازي.
And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Then the veil is lifted and they look at the face of Allah, mighty and majestic' (1) and his saying: 'The most honored among them to Allah is the one who looks at His face' (2). His saying: 'And for its occurrence there are consensus (ijma') and textual evidence (nass). As for consensus (3), there is no doubt that proving the occurrence of seeing (ru'ya) is only possible through scriptural evidence. They have argued for it by consensus and textual evidence. As for consensus, it is the agreement of the community before the emergence of dissenters on the occurrence of seeing (ru'ya). And that the verses and hadiths about it are to be taken in their apparent meaning, such that the hadith of seeing (ru'ya) was transmitted by twenty-one of the major companions. As for textual evidence, from the Book is the saying of the Most High: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (4). Indeed, 'looking' (nazar) connected with 'ila' (to) is either in the sense of seeing (ru'ya), or that which necessarily implies it, according to transmitted testimony from the imams of language and tracking its usages; or it is a metaphor for it because it is an expression of turning the pupil toward the visible object in seeking to see it. But here the literal meaning is impossible due to the impossibility of facing and direction, so seeing (ru'ya) is determined because it is the closest of metaphors, to the point that it joins the realities by the testimony of common usage. And the placing of 'looking' before 'to their Lord' is merely for emphasis and observance of rhyme, not for exclusivity; or for affirmation of exclusivity in a figurative sense, meaning that the believers, due to their absorption in contemplating His beauty and their fixation on the greatness of His majesty, are as if they do not turn to anything else and see only Allah. And it was objected that 'ila' here is not a particle but a noun meaning 'favor' (ni'mah), one of the 'ala' (favors), and 'nadhirah' is from 'nazar' meaning 'waiting' (intidhar), as in His saying: 'Look at us (andhiruna) so that we may borrow from your light' (5). And even if granted, the phrase connected with 'ila' may also come in the sense of waiting, as in the poet's saying: 'Faces looking (nadhirah) on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful—success comes' and 'Disheveled ones looking at Bilal as the thirsty look at the rain cloud' and 'All creatures look at his generosity as pilgrims look at the rising crescent.' And even if granted, then 'looking' connected with 'ila' is not in the sense of seeing (ru'ya), nor a necessary concomitant thereof (1), because it is described by what seeing is not described with, such as intensity, turning away, satisfaction, turning, humility, and abasement; and seeing can be absent while it is present, like: 'I looked at the crescent but did not see it.' And for its verification alongside (the absence of seeing), Allah Most High said: 'And you see them looking at you, but they do not see' (2). And making it a metaphor for seeing is not better than interpreting it as a deleted genitive construction, i.e., 'looking at the reward of their Lord' as mentioned by Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and many exegetes. In summary, there is no doubt that what we have mentioned are possibilities that repel the argument from the verse. And it was answered that the context of the verse is to give glad tidings to the believers and to show that they will be in extreme joy and happiness on that day. Informing about their waiting for favor and reward does not suit that; rather, it might contradict it, because with waiting there is 'red death' (i.e., extreme anxiety), so one is more deserving of grief, sorrow, worry, and distress. Even if certainty of attainment is assumed. As for the idea that 'ila' is a noun meaning 'favor'—even if established in the language, there is no doubt about its remoteness, strangeness, and causing confusion in understanding when 'looking' is attached to it. For this reason, no one among the early exegetes of the first and second centuries interpreted the verse accordingly; rather, they agreed on the contrary. And the view that 'looking' connected with 'ila'—especially when attributed to the face—means 'waiting' is not established by reliable authorities, nor do the verses indicate it, because it can be interpreted as turning the pupil with interpretations that are not hidden. As for considering a deleted genitive construction, it is a departure from the literal or well-known metaphorical meaning in deletion, where there is no clue to determine the deleted element. The full discussion of the objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the argument from this verse and the refutation of them by the people of truth is mentioned in 'Nihayat al-Uqul' (6) of Imam al-Razi.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Sunni doctrine of ru'ya (seeing Allah in the afterlife) based on the Quranic verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' and a refutation of Mu'tazilite objections. The author begins by stating that the occurrence of ru'ya can only be proven through scriptural evidence (sam'iyyat), not reason. He then presents two scriptural proofs: consensus (ijma') of the early community, and the Quranic text itself. For the verse, he explains that the word 'nazar' (looking) connected with 'ila' must be interpreted as either seeing (ru'ya) or its necessary concomitant, because the literal meaning of turning the pupil is impossible here due to Allah's transcendence beyond direction. Thus, ru'ya is the closest metaphorical meaning. He also discusses the syntactic emphasis and the believers' absorption in beholding Allah. Then, the author lists Mu'tazilite objections: (1) that 'ila' is a noun meaning 'favor', not a particle, so 'nadhirah' means 'looking for favor'; (2) even if 'ila' is a particle, it can indicate 'waiting' as in poetry; (3) 'nazar' with 'ila' does not necessarily imply seeing (as in looking at something without seeing it); (4) it could be an ellipsis meaning 'looking at the reward of their Lord'. The author responds: the context of the verse is glad tidings and joy, which contradicts the anxiety of waiting; the interpretation of 'ila' as 'favor' is linguistically far-fetched and was never adopted by early exegetes; the idea of 'waiting' is not established for this phrasing; and the ellipsis interpretation is a departure from the clear meaning without a clue. He concludes by referring to al-Razi's 'Nihayat al-Uqul' for a fuller treatment.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
إجماع (ijma')
نظر (nazar)
إلى (ila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ru'ya can be proven rationally without revelation.
Correction: Al-Taftazani emphasizes that ru'ya is a scriptural matter (sam'iyyat), not knowable through reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does 'nazar' with 'ila' in the verse necessarily imply ru'ya?
A) Because it is the literal meaning of the words.
B) Because the literal meaning (turning the pupil) is impossible for Allah, and ru'ya is the closest metaphor.
C) Because the consensus of the companions supports this interpretation.
D) Because the verse is about glad tidings and joy.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni proof for ru'ya rests on scriptural evidence (consensus and the Quranic verse), where linguistic analysis shows that 'nazar ila' must be interpreted as 'seeing' because the literal meaning is impossible for Allah.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Objections to the Ru'ya Verse
Al-Taftazani refutes four Mu'tazilite objections to the verse, providing linguistic and contextual rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main Mu'tazilite objections to the Quranic proof for ru'ya and the Sunni responses, particularly the linguistic and contextual arguments that refute alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite interpretations of the verse are linguistically strained and contradict the joyful context of the afterlife.

In the second part of his discussion, al-Taftazani presents and refutes four objections raised by the Mu'tazila against using the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' as proof for ru'ya. The objections range from alternative meanings of 'ila' and 'nazar' to claims of ellipsis. Al-Taftazani's responses demonstrate that the Mu'tazilite readings are linguistically implausible and contradict the clear context of the verse as glad tidings.

Guided Path
1
Learn the first objection: 'ila' as a noun meaning favor
The Mu'tazila claim 'ila' is a noun (ni'mah), so 'nadhirah' means 'looking for favor'. Al-Taftazani responds that this is far-fetched and no early exegete used it, and the context of glad tidings contradicts waiting.
This objection attacks the grammatical basis of the proof.
2
Learn the second objection: 'nazar ila' meaning waiting
They cite poetic usage where 'nazar ila' means 'wait for'. The response: this usage is not authentically established for this construction, and the verse's joy is incompatible with anxious waiting.
Even if certain examples exist, the context overrides.
3
Learn the third objection: 'nazar ila' does not imply seeing
Examples like looking at the moon without seeing it, and the Quranic verse 'and you see them looking at you while they do not see' are given. Al-Taftazani counters that these do not negate the intrinsic link in the context of Allah; the metaphor of seeing remains the closest.
The objection uses empirical examples, but theological context differs.
4
Learn the fourth objection: ellipsis (looking at reward)
They propose the deleted genitive construction: 'looking at the reward of their Lord'. Al-Taftazani rejects this as a departure from clear meaning without contextual clue.
This objection is refuted by the principle of assuming no ellipsis without evidence.

And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Then the veil is lifted and they look at the face of Allah, mighty and majestic' (1) and his saying: 'The most honored among them to Allah is the one who looks at His face' (2). Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Sunni doctrine of ru'ya (seeing Allah in the afterlife) based on the Quranic verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' and a refutation of Mu'tazilite objections. According to the passage, what is the primary response to the Mu'tazilite claim that 'ila' in the verse means 'favor' (ni'mah)?
A) It is not linguistically established.
B) It is far-fetched and no early exegete used it.
C) The context of glad tidings contradicts waiting.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objections to the ru'ya verse are refuted by showing their linguistic weakness and inconsistency with the verse's joyful context, confirming the Sunni interpretation of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazilite objections, do you find any of them appealing to your own rational mind? How does Al-Taftazani's refutation challenge not just their arguments but your own reliance on speculative reasoning over the plain meaning of revelation?
?
The verse is described as a glad tiding—a promise of joy. Do you genuinely feel anticipation and joy at the thought of seeing your Lord, or has the promise become abstract and distant? What would need to change in your heart to make you yearn for that moment right now?
?
Al-Taftazani defends the literal meaning using linguistics and context. Are you willing to accept the clear meaning of Allah's words even when your intellect raises objections? Where in your own life do you find yourself rationalizing away clear commands or promises of the Quran?

The Mu'tazila's First Objection: The Argument from Facing
Taftazani presents the Mu'tazila's first intellectual argument against ru'ya: the objection from muqabala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first rational argument against the vision of Allah (ru'ya), based on the necessity of facing (muqabala) for any act of vision.

Core Idea
If Allah were visible, He would have to be facing the seer—an impossibility for the transcendent Creator.

The debate over ru'ya (seeing Allah) involves both scriptural and rational arguments. The Mu'tazila, who denied the possibility of vision, presented several intellectual doubts. The first and most foundational is the 'doubt of facing' (shubhat al-muqabala), which claims that vision requires the object to be in front of the seer. Taftazani here presents this argument and its logical consequences, which the Mu'tazila use to prove the impossibility of seeing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the author's structured presentation of the opponent's argument.
The text cuts off mid-sentence; we only have the first part of this objection.
2
Identify the core premise
The main claim is that if Allah were visible, He would necessarily be facing the seer. Identify how the Mu'tazila define 'facing' and why they consider it necessary for vision.
They argue from direct vision and vision via mirrors.
3
Trace the derived absurdities
The passage lists several impossible consequences if Allah were facing: He would be in a direction, be a substance/accident, be located, be finite, etc. Pick out at least three of these and understand why they are considered impossible for Allah.
These consequences are meant to show the absurdity of the original assumption.
4
Reflect on the argument's strategy
Consider the argument's structure: it is not a direct scriptural proof but a rational demonstration. Reflect on why this kind of argument was central to the Mu'tazila's rejection of ru'ya.
This argument does not depend on specific verses but on the nature of vision itself.

1 - أدلة المخالف على عدم الرؤية قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه . الأول: أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان بالضرورة مقابلاً فكان في جهة جوهراً أو عرضاً . قوله - تمسك المخالف بوجوه . يعني للمعتزلة شبه عقلية وسمعية بعضها يمنع صحة الرؤية، وبعضها وقوعها فالعقلية أصولها ثلاث: الأولى : شبهة المقابلة وهي أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان مقابلاً للرائي حقيقة كما في الرؤية بالذات، أو حكماً لما في الرؤية بالمرآة. والحق أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا التفصيل. . لأن المرئي بالمرآة هو الصورة المنطبعة فيها المقابلة للرائي حقيقة لاماله الصورة كالوجه مثلاً، ويدعون في لزوم المقابلة الضرورة ويفرعون على ذلك وجوهاً من الاستدلال مثل أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان في جهة وحيز وهو محال ، ولكان جوهراً أو عرضاً لأن المتحيز جوهر وبالتبعية عرض. ولكان إما في البدن أو خارج البدن أو فيهما ، ولكان في الجنة أو خارج الجنة أو فيهما . إذ لا تعقل الرؤية إن لم تكن فيه ولا خارجة لانتفاء المقابلة، ولكان المرئي إما كله فيكون محدوداً متناهياً أو بعضه فيكون مبعضاً متجزئاً، وهذا بخلاف العلم، فإنه إنما يتعلق بالصفات، ولا فساد في أن يكون المعلوم كلها أو بعضها، ولكان إما على مسافة من الرائي فيكون في حيز وجهة أو فيكون في العين أو متصلاً بها، ولكان رؤية المؤمنين إياه، إما دفعة فيكون متصلاً بعين كل أحد بتمامه فيتكثر أو لا بتمامه فيتجزيء ، أو منفصلاً عنها فيكون على مسافة ، وإما على التعاقب مع استوائهم في سلامة الحواس فيلزم الحجاب بالنسبة إلى
1 – Evidence of the opponent against the vision. He said: The opponent clings to aspects. The first: that if He were visible, He would necessarily be facing, so He would be in a direction, a substance or an accident. His statement: 'The opponent clings to aspects' – meaning for the Mu'tazila there are intellectual and scriptural doubts, some prevent the validity of vision, and some prevent its occurrence. The intellectual ones have three foundations: The first is the doubt of facing, which is that if He were visible, He would be facing the seer truly as in direct vision, or virtually as in vision through a mirror. The truth is that there is no need for this detail, because the visible in the mirror is the image imprinted in it truly facing the seer, not his image like the face, for example. And they claim necessity in the requirement of facing, and they branch from that several aspects of inference, such as: if He were visible, He would be in a direction and space, which is impossible; and He would be a substance or accident because that which occupies space is a substance and, by extension, an accident; and He would be either inside the body, outside it, or both; and He would be either in Paradise, outside of it, or both – since vision is inconceivable if it is not in it nor outside it due to the lack of facing; and the visible would be either entirely, thus limited and finite, or partially, thus divisible – and this is unlike knowledge, for it only relates to attributes, and there is no corruption in that the known is all or some of it; and He would be either at a distance from the seer, thus in a place and direction, or in the eye or connected to it; and the believers' vision of Him would be either all at once, so He would be connected to each one's eye entirely, thus He would be multiplied, or not entirely, thus He would be divided; or separate from them, thus at a distance; or in succession while they are equal in soundness of senses, so a veil would be necessary relative to...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the first intellectual objection of the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author (Taftazani) introduces the opponent's arguments, noting they are both intellectual and scriptural. The intellectual arguments have three foundations; the first is the 'doubt of facing' (shubhat al-muqabala). The argument: if Allah were visible, He would have to be facing the seer, either directly (as in real vision) or virtually (as in a mirror). The author comments that the mirror example is unnecessary because the reflected image is itself a real facing image. The Mu'tazila claim that facing is necessary for vision, and from this they derive multiple absurdities: if facing, then Allah would occupy space (hayyiz), making Him a substance or accident; He would be located inside or outside the body; in or out of Paradise; He would be either finite or divisible; He would have to be at a distance or in the eye; if many believers see Him simultaneously, He would either be multiplied or divided; or if they see Him successively, some would be veiled. All these are impossible for Allah, so they conclude vision is impossible. The text cuts off mid-sentence at 'فيلزم الحجاب بالنسبة إلى' (so a veil would be necessary relative to...). This is an standard Mu'tazili argument based on their physical account of vision.

Key Terms
شبهة المقابلة
الرؤية
الجهة
الحيز
الجوهر
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila only used scriptural arguments to deny ru'ya.
Correction: This passage shows they used rational arguments, specifically the argument from facing, which is based on the nature of vision.

Checkpoint
What is the first intellectual argument (shubha) presented by the Mu'tazila against the vision of Allah?
A) The argument from scriptural contradiction
B) The argument from facing (muqabala)
C) The argument from divine simplicity
D) The argument from infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first rational objection to ru'ya is the argument from facing, which claims that vision requires the object to be in front of the seer, leading to impossible implications for a transcendent God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine ‘seeing Allah’ in the Hereafter, do you unconsciously picture a form or direction? How does the Mu'tazila's argument expose a hidden anthropomorphism in your own heart? What must you do to purify your expectation of ru'ya from any trace of limitation?
?
This argument claims that vision forces the object to be in a place. You believe Allah is above space, yet do you truly treat His presence in your life as beyond location? When you turn to Him in prayer, do you feel you are turning toward a direction? How can you deepen your sense of His nearness without confining Him?
?
The Mu'tazila used reason to defend Allah's transcendence—but their conclusion denied a promise of the Prophet. Have you ever let a logical argument override a clear text because it seemed more sophisticated? Where in your faith do you need to humble your intellect before revelation, even when the reasoning feels compelling?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
