The Mu'tazilite Doubt of Opposition Against Seeing Allah
Mu'tazilite rational objection based on necessary opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational argument that if Allah were visible, He would have to be opposite the seer, implying direction, location, and composition, and the various consequences they derive.

Core Idea
Vision requires opposition; Allah cannot be opposite, so He cannot be seen.

The Mu'tazila argued that vision is only possible when the seen object is opposite the seer. Since Allah is not in a direction or location, He cannot be seen. This lesson presents their first rational doubt—shubhat al-muqabala—and the detailed consequences they derived from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's core premise
Read the first few lines where the Mu'tazilite claim is stated: 'if He were visible, He would necessarily be opposite' and then they explain what that entails.
Note that they claim this is a necessary (daruri) truth.
2
Identify the branching arguments
The text lists several consequences: He would be in a direction/space, be a substance/accident, be limited/divisible, etc. Underline each.
These are all meant to show impossibility.
3
Understand the analogy with mirror vision
Notice the detail about mirror vision: the image in the mirror is actually opposite the seer. This is used to strengthen their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه . الأول: أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان بالضرورة مقابلاً فكان في جهة جوهراً أو عرضاً . يعني للمعتزلة شبه عقلية وسمعية بعضها يمنع صحة الرؤية، وبعضها وقوعها فالعقلية أصولها ثلاث: بالاستقلال الأولى : شبهة المقابلة وهي أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان مقابلاً للرائي حقيقة كما في الرؤية بالذات، أو حكماً لما في الرؤية بالمرآة. والحق أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا التفصيل. لأن المرئي بالمرآة هو الصورة المنطبعة فيها المقابلة للرائي حقيقة لاماله الصورة كالوجه مثلاً، ويدعون في لزوم المقابلة الضرورة ويفرعون على ذلك وجوهاً من الاستدلال مثل أنه لو كان مرئياً لكان في جهة وحيز وهو محال ، ولكان جوهراً أو عرضاً لأن المتحيز جوهر وبالتبعية عرض. ولكان إما في البدن أو خارج البدن أو فيهما ، ولكان في الجنة أو خارج الجنة أو فيهما . إذ لا تعقل الرؤية إن لم تكن فيه ولا خارجة لانتفاء المقابلة، ولكان المرئي إما كله فيكون محدوداً متناهياً أو بعضه فيكون مبعضاً متجزئاً، وهذا بخلاف العلم، فإنه إنما يتعلق بالصفات، ولا فساد في أن يكون المعلوم كلها أو بعضها، ولكان إما على مسافة من الرائي فيكون في حيز وجهة أو فيكون في العين أو متصلاً بها، ولكان رؤية المؤمنين إياه، إما دفعة فيكون متصلاً بعين كل أحد بتمامه فيتكثر أو لا بتمامه فيتجزيء ، أو منفصلاً عنها فيكون على مسافة ، وإما على التعاقب مع استوائهم في سلامة الحواس فيلزم الحجاب بالنسبة إلى البعض، ولكان رؤيته إما مع رؤية شيء آخر مما في الجنة فيكون على جهة منه ضرورة. إن رؤية الشيئين دفعة لا تعقل إلا كذلك، وإما لا معها فيكون ما هو باطن في الدارين مرئياً وما هو ظاهر غير مرئي مع شرائط الرؤية، وحديث غلبة شعاع أحد المرئيين إنما يصح في الأجسام. والجواب : أن لزوم المقابلة والجهة ،ممنوع ، وإنما الرؤية نوع من الإدراك يخلقه الله تعالى متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء، ودعوى الضرورة فما نازع فيه جم الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموع . ولو سلم في الشاهد ، فلا يلزم في الغائب لأن الرؤيتين مختلفتان ، إما بالماهية ، وإما بالهوية لا محالة، فيجوز اختلافهما في الشروط واللوازم وهذا هو المراد بالرؤية بلا كيف بمعنى خلوها عن الشرائط، والكيفيات المعتبرة في رؤية الأجسام والأعراض ، لا بمعنى خلو الرؤية أو الرائي أو المرئي عن جميع الحالات والصفات على ما يفهمه أرباب الجهالات، فيعترضون بأن الرؤية فعل من أفعال العبد، أو كسب من أكسابه فبالضرورة يكون واقعاً بصفة من الصفات، وكذا المرني بحاسة العين، لا بد أن يكون له كيفية من الكيفيات. نعم يتوجه أن يقال : نزاعنا إنما هو في هذا النوع من الرؤية ، لا في الرؤية المخالفة لها بالحقيقة المسماة عندكم بالانكشاف التام، وعندنا بالعلم الضروري.
He said: The opponent holds to arguments. First: That if He were visible, He would necessarily be opposite, so He would be in a direction, a substance or an accident. That is, for the Mu'tazila there are rational and transmitted doubts, some preventing the validity of vision, and some its occurrence. The rational ones have three principles: independently. The first: the doubt of opposition, which is that if He were visible, He would be opposite the seer in reality, as in vision by essence, or by judgment, as in vision via a mirror. The truth is that there is no need for this detail, because what is seen in a mirror is the image impressed in it, actually opposite the seer, not his image like the face, for example. They claim necessity in the requirement of opposition, and they branch out from that various proofs, such that if He were visible, He would be in a direction and space, which is impossible; and He would be a substance or an accident because that which occupies space is a substance and secondarily an accident; and He would either be in the body or outside the body or in both; and He would be in Paradise or outside Paradise or in both, since vision is inconceivable unless it is in it or outside it due to the absence of opposition; and the visible would either be entirely, thus limited and finite, or partially, thus partitioned and divisible—this unlike knowledge, for it relates to attributes, and there is no corruption in that the known is all or part of it; and He would either be at a distance from the seer, thus in a space and direction, or in the eye or connected to it; and the believers' vision of Him would be either all at once, so He would be connected to each person's eye in His entirety and thus multiply, or not in His entirety and thus divide, or separate from it and thus be at a distance; or sequentially while they are equal in soundness of senses, necessitating a veil for some; and His vision would be either together with the vision of something else from Paradise, thus He would be in a direction from it necessarily—indeed the simultaneous vision of two things is inconceivable except thus—or not with it, so that which is interior in both abodes would be visible and that which is exterior invisible despite the conditions of vision, and the talk of the overpowering of the ray of one of the two visible objects is only valid for bodies. And the answer: That the requirement of opposition and direction is denied; rather, vision is a type of perception that Allah Exalted creates whenever He wills and for whatever He wills. And the claim of necessity—what the great multitude of rational disputants have disputed is not heard. And even if it is granted in the witnessed, it does not follow in the absent because the two visions are different, either in essence or in identity unavoidably, so it is permissible for them to differ in conditions and concomitants. And this is what is meant by vision without 'how', meaning its being free from the conditions and qualities considered in the vision of bodies and accidents, not meaning the vision, the seer, or the seen being free from all states and attributes, as understood by people of ignorance, who object that vision is an act of the servant or an acquisition of his, so necessarily it occurs with a quality, and likewise the seen through the eye's sense must have a quality. Yes, it may be said: Our dispute is only about this type of vision, not about the vision differing from it in reality, called by you perfect disclosure and by us necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite rational objections against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) and Taftazani's refutation. The objection is structured around the 'doubt of opposition' (shubhat al-muqabala): the claim that if Allah were visible, He would have to be opposite the seer, implying direction, location, and composition—all impossible for Allah. The opponent then derives several consequences: He would be in a direction and space, thus a substance or accident; He would be limited in body or part of it; His simultaneous vision by all believers would entail multiplicity or division; and so on. Taftazani's answer is twofold: First, he denies the premise that vision necessarily requires opposition and direction; vision is a type of perception that Allah creates directly without these conditions. The opponent's claim of necessity is not conceded, especially since many rational thinkers dispute it. Second, even if such conditions held in worldly vision, they do not apply to the unseen (gha'ib) because the two visions differ in essence or identity, so their conditions and concomitants may differ. This is the meaning of 'vision without how' (ru'ya bila kayf): free from the conditions of seeing bodies and accidents, not free from all states. Taftazani clarifies that the dispute is specifically about this type of vision (the beatific vision), not about a purely metaphorical 'disclosure'.

Key Terms
رؤية
مقابلة
جهة
جوهر
عرض
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the first Mu'tazilite rational argument against the vision of Allah?
A) Vision requires a physical eye.
B) If Allah were visible, He would necessarily be opposite the seer.
C) Allah cannot be seen because He is not a body.
D) Vision is a contingent attribute.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argued that vision necessitates opposition, leading to impossible consequences for Allah, but this premise is not conceded by Sunni scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on understanding a truth about Allah entirely through your own reason, like the Mu'tazilites did? Will you admit that your mind has limits and accept what Allah has promised, even if it seems impossible to your intellect?
?
The Mu'tazilites feared that affirming vision would lower Allah to the level of creation. Do you realize that this very fear can become a veil between you and the longing to see your Lord? Are you willing to yearn for what your mind cannot fully grasp?
?
If you were to apply the Mu'tazilite method of reasoning to other matters of the unseen, what would you be forced to deny? Are you prepared to submit to revelation when your intellect objects, trusting that Allah knows what you do not?

Divine Attributes

Taftazani's Refutation and the Meaning of Vision Without How
Taftazani's refutation of the opposition argument and definition of 'bila kayf'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's two-part refutation of the Mu'tazilite opposition argument: denying the necessary link between vision and opposition, and distinguishing between witnessed and unseen vision, which leads to the correct meaning of 'vision without how'.

Core Idea
Vision does not require opposition; even if it did, the unseen differs from the witnessed.

After presenting the Mu'tazilite doubt of opposition, Taftazani provides a two-pronged response. First, he denies that vision necessarily requires opposition or direction—Allah creates vision directly without such conditions. Second, he argues that even if those conditions held in worldly vision, they need not apply to the unseen. This leads to the proper understanding of 'vision without how' as freedom from physical conditions, not from all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation's first part
Read from 'والجواب' to 'غير مسموع'. Taftazani denies the necessity of opposition and direction, stating that vision is a perception Allah creates as He wills.
The claim of necessity is rejected because many rational thinkers dispute it.
2
Read the second part
Read from 'ولو سلم' to 'عندكم بالانكشاف التام'. He grants for argument's sake that conditions apply in the witnessed, but argues they don't transfer to the unseen because the two visions differ in essence or identity.
3
Understand 'bila kayf'
Taftazani clarifies that 'vision without how' means free from the conditions of physical sight (opposition, direction, etc.), not free from all attributes or states.
He warns against the ignorant who think it means the vision or seer has no qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: The opponent holds to arguments. First: That if He were visible, He would necessarily be opposite, so He would be in a direction, a substance or an accident. That is, for the Mu'tazila there are rational and transmitted doubts, some preventing the validity of vision, and some its occurrence. The rational ones have three principles: independently. The first: the doubt of opposition, which is that if He were visible, He would be opposite the seer in reality, as in vision by essence, or by judgment, as in vision via a mirror. The truth is that there is no need for this detail, because what is seen in a mirror is the image impressed in it, actually opposite the seer, not his image like the face, for example. They claim necessity in the requirement of opposition, and they branch out from that various proofs, such that if He were visible, He would be in a direction and space, which is impossible; and He would be a substance or an accident because that which occupies space is a substance and secondarily an accident; and He would either be in the body or outside the body or in both; and He would be in Paradise or outside Paradise or in both, since vision is inconceivable unless it is in it or outside it due to the absence of opposition; and the visible would either be entirely, thus limited and finite, or partially, thus partitioned and divisible—this unlike knowledge, for it relates to attributes, and there is no corruption in that the known is all or part of it; and He would either be at a distance from the seer, thus in a space and direction, or in the eye or connected to it; and the believers' vision of Him would be either all at once, so He would be connected to each person's eye in His entirety and thus multiply, or not in His entirety and thus divide, or separate from it and thus be at a distance; or sequentially while they are equal in soundness of senses, necessitating a veil for some; and His vision would be either together with the vision of something else from Paradise, thus He would be in a direction from it necessarily—indeed the simultaneous vision of two things is inconceivable except thus—or not with it, so that which is interior in both abodes would be visible and that which is exterior invisible despite the conditions of vision, and the talk of the overpowering of the ray of one of the two visible objects is only valid for bodies. And the answer: That the requirement of opposition and direction is denied; rather, vision is a type of perception that Allah Exalted creates whenever He wills and for whatever He wills. And the claim of necessity—what the great multitude of rational disputants have disputed is not heard. And even if it is granted in the witnessed, it does not follow in the absent because the two visions are different, either in essence or in identity unavoidably, so it is permissible for them to differ in conditions and concomitants. And this is what is meant by vision without 'how', meaning its being free from the conditions and qualities considered in the vision of bodies and accidents, not meaning the vision, the seer, or the seen being free from all states and attributes, as understood by people of ignorance, who object that vision is an act of the servant or an acquisition of his, so necessarily it occurs with a quality, and likewise the seen through the eye's sense must have a quality. Yes, it may be said: Our dispute is only about this type of vision, not about the vision differing from it in reality, called by you perfect disclosure and by us necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite rational objections against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) and Taftazani's refutation. The objection is structured around the 'doubt of opposition' (shubhat al-muqabala): the claim that if Allah were visible, He would have to be opposite the seer, implying direction, location, and composition—all impossible for Allah. The opponent then derives several consequences: He would be in a direction and space, thus a substance or accident; He would be limited in body or part of it; His simultaneous vision by all believers would entail multiplicity or division; and so on. Taftazani's answer is twofold: First, he denies the premise that vision necessarily requires opposition and direction; vision is a type of perception that Allah creates directly without these conditions. The opponent's claim of necessity is not conceded, especially since many rational thinkers dispute it. Second, even if such conditions held in worldly vision, they do not apply to the unseen (gha'ib) because the two visions differ in essence or identity, so their conditions and concomitants may differ. This is the meaning of 'vision without how' (ru'ya bila kayf): free from the conditions of seeing bodies and accidents, not free from all states. Taftazani clarifies that the dispute is specifically about this type of vision (the beatific vision), not about a purely metaphorical 'disclosure'.

Key Terms

بلا كيف
غائب

انكشاف تام
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'vision without how' means the vision has no attribute or quality at all.
Correction: Taftazani explains it means free from the specific conditions of physical sight, not free from all states or attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what does 'vision without how' (ru'ya bila kayf) mean?
A) The vision has no quality or attribute at all.
B) The seer has no physical eye.
C) Free from the conditions and qualities of physical sight.
D) It is a metaphorical disclosure.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazilite opposition argument by denying the necessity of opposition and distinguishing between the conditions of worldly vision and the beatific vision, defining 'bila kayf' correctly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate seeing Allah on the Day of Judgement, do you find yourself trying to imagine 'how'? Are you willing to train your heart to long for the vision without needing to understand its modality?
?
The Mu'tazilites denied the beatific vision because they measured Allah by the conditions of this world. Have you ever limited Allah in your mind by your own experiences—assuming He must act or appear in a way you can comprehend? Where in your life do you need to repent of that limitation and affirm His absolute freedom?
?
Taftazani says the true vision is a real perception Allah creates directly. Does your heart truly thirst for that vision as the greatest reward, or are you more attached to worldly pleasures? What concrete change in your priorities would prove that you value seeing Allah above all else?

The Third Doubt: The Doubt of Obstacles and Its Answer
The author states and refutes the third doubt about the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt against the vision of Allah and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Core Idea
The conditions for seeing physical objects don't automatically apply to seeing Allah.

This passage presents the third doubt (shubha al-mawani') about whether Allah can be seen. The doubt argues that if vision were possible, it would be continuous. The author refutes it by showing that the conditions for vision in this world are not necessarily transferable to the unseen, and that certainty about non-existence can come from customary knowledge rather than logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the doubt is stated. Notice the argument: if vision were possible, it would be continuous, but it's not, so vision is impossible.
2
Identify the author's response
The author distinguishes two senses of 'possibility' (logical possibility vs rational certainty) and denies the necessity of the conditional.
3
Understand the analogy
The author uses the example of a mountain of ruby to show that our certainty of non-existence comes from customary knowledge, not from a logical rule about vision.
4
Note the key concession
The author concedes that for physical objects, vision is necessary when conditions are met, but for the unseen, we do not concede that because the vision may differ in essence or require an additional power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله - الثالث : الشبهة الثالثة شبهة الموانع وهي أنه لو جازت رؤيته لدامت لكل سليم الحاسة في الدنيا والآخرة، فلزم أن نراه الآن وفي الجنة على الدوام، والأول منتف بالضرورة، والثاني بالإجماع، وبالنصوص القاطعة الدالة على اشتغالهم بغير ذلك من اللذات . وجه اللزوم أنه يكفي للرؤية في حق الغائب سلامة الحاسة وكون الشيء جائز الرؤية، لأن المقابلة وانتفاء الموانع من فرط الصغر أو اللطافة أو القرب أو البعد وحيلولة الحجاب الكثيف أو الشعاع المناسب لضوء العين، إنما يشترط في الشاهد أعني رؤية الأجسام والأعراض، فعند تحقق الأمرين لو لم تجب الرؤية لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة لا نراها لأن الله تعالى لم يخلق رؤيتها أو لتوقفها على شرط آخر، وهذا قطعي البطلان. والجواب : أنه إن أريد جواز ذلك في نفسه بمعنى كونه من الأمور الممكنة فليس قطعي البطلان بل قطعي الصحة والشرطية المذكورة ليست لزومية بل اتفاقية بمنزلة قولنا : لو لم تجب الرؤية عند تحقق الشرائط لكان العالم ممكناً، وإن أريد جوازه عند العقل بمعنى تجويزه ثبوت الجبال، وعدم جزمه بانتفائها، فاللزوم ممنوع . فإن انتفاءها من العاديات القطعية الضرورية كعدم جبل من الياقوت، وبحر من الزئبق، ونحو ذلك مما يخلق الله تعالى العلم الضروري بانتفائها، وإن كان ثبوتها الممكنات دون المحالات وليس الجزم به مبنياً على العلم بأنه تجب الرؤية عند وجود شرائطها، لحصوله من غير ملاحظة ذلك، بل مع الجهل . بذلك سلمنا من وجوب الرؤية عند تحقق الشرائط المذكورة في حق الشاهد، لكن لا نسلم وجوبها في الغائب عند تحقق الأمرين لجواز أن تكون الرؤيتان مختلفتين في الماهية فيختلفان في اللوازم أو تكون رؤية الخالق مشروطة بزيادة قوة إدراكية في الباصرة لا يخلقها الله تعالى إلا في الجنة في بعض الأوقات
His saying: 'The third: The third doubt is the doubt of obstacles, which is that if His vision were permissible, it would be continuous for every sound-sensed person in this world and the hereafter, so it would necessarily follow that we see Him now and in Paradise perpetually. The first is necessarily false, and the second is false by consensus and by definitive texts indicating their occupation with other pleasures. The aspect of necessity is that for vision in the case of the unseen, soundness of the sense and the thing being visible suffice, because facing and absence of obstacles—such as excessive smallness, subtlety, proximity, distance, interposition of a thick veil, or radiation suitable to the light of the eye—are only required in the witnessed, i.e., the vision of bodies and accidents. So when these two matters are realized, if vision were not obligatory, it would be possible that there were towering mountains before us that we do not see because God did not create our vision of them or because it depends on another condition, and this is definitively false. The answer: If what is meant is the possibility of that in itself, meaning it being among possible matters, then it is not definitively false but definitively true, and the mentioned conditional is not necessary but coincidental, like our saying: 'If vision were not obligatory when conditions are realized, the world would be possible.' And if what is meant is its possibility according to reason, meaning its deeming the existence of mountains possible and not being certain of their non-existence, then the necessity is denied. For their non-existence is among the customary necessary things, like the non-existence of a mountain of ruby or a sea of mercury, and the like, for which God creates necessary knowledge of their non-existence, even though their existence is among possibilities, not impossibilities, and certainty about it is not based on knowledge that vision is obligatory when its conditions exist, because it occurs without noticing that, but rather with ignorance. By that we concede the obligation of vision when the mentioned conditions are realized in the case of the witnessed, but we do not concede its obligation in the unseen when the two matters are realized, because it is possible that the two visions differ in essence and thus differ in their concomitants, or that the vision of the Creator is conditioned by an additional perceptual power in the sight that God only creates in Paradise at some times.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third doubt (shubha al-mawani') against the possibility of seeing Allah. The doubt argues: if seeing Allah were possible, it would have to be continuous for every healthy person, which is false (since we don't see Him now, and in Paradise the blessed are occupied with other pleasures). The alleged necessary connection is based on assuming that only two conditions are needed: a sound sense and a visible object. The author answers by distinguishing two senses of 'possibility.' If the doubt means logical possibility, the conditional ('if vision were not obligatory when conditions are met, then it would be possible not to see mountains') is indeed true, but that doesn't disprove the vision's possibility; it only shows that God could withhold vision for some reason. If the doubt means rational certainty (i.e., reason judges it impossible not to see when conditions are met), then the author denies the necessity: we often are certain of non-existence without reflecting on necessary vision, as in the case of a mountain of ruby—our certainty comes from customary knowledge, not from a logical rule. The author then concedes that for physical objects, when conditions are met, vision is necessary in the witnessed world, but for the unseen (gha'ib), we do not concede that same necessity because the vision of Allah may differ in essence or may require an additional perceptual power granted only in Paradise. Thus the doubt fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if a thing is possibly visible, it must be seen when the sense is sound, ignoring that conditions may differ between physical and divine vision.
Correction: The author shows that for the unseen, vision may require additional conditions (e.g., a special perceptual power) and that our certainty of non-existence can be based on customary knowledge, not logical necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point the author makes in answer to the third doubt?
A) Vision of Allah is impossible because we don't see Him now.
B) The conditions for vision in the witnessed world do not necessarily apply to the unseen, because the vision may differ in essence or require additional power.
C) The doubt is based on a false premise because we do see Allah in this world.
D) The doubt is valid, so we must affirm that Allah is seen only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt fails because the conditions for vision in the physical world do not necessarily apply to seeing Allah, and our certainty about non-existence can come from customary knowledge, not logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that Allah must act according to your logic (like the doubt's conditional), do you realize that you are imposing limitations on the Creator? In what area of your life have you unconsciously judged Allah by created standards?
?
This lesson shows that our certainty about everyday things (like no mountains before us) comes from God's custom, not our own reasoning. When you feel sure about something in your faith, is your trust in your own intellect or in Allah's guarantee? Will you pray today for Allah to grant you 'necessary knowledge' of His oneness, not just intellectual conclusions?
?
The author says that divine vision may require an 'additional perceptual power' only granted in Paradise. Are you living today as if seeing Allah is your ultimate goal, preparing your heart for that vision by seeking Him now through obedience? Or have you become content with worldly pleasures, forgetting that the vision is the greatest reward?

Al-Razi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Vision Conditions
The author refutes the Mu'tazila claim of identical eyes and presents al-Razi's two arguments against limiting conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weaknesses in the Mu'tazila claim about the similarity of worldly and hereafter vision, and al-Razi's two arguments that challenge the limitation of conditions for vision.

Core Idea
Vision conditions are not limited; distance and density affect visibility.

The Mu'tazila argued that since the two eyes are similar, conditions for vision must be identical and limited. Al-Razi countered with two empirical arguments showing additional factors like distance and density affect visibility. We'll examine these arguments and the responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Start by reading the Mu'tazila assertion that the worldly and hereafter eyes are similar, so their conditions and obstacles must be identical and limited to those mentioned.
Notice they rely on a definitive correlation (al-dawran al-qat'i).
2
Study al-Razi's first argument
Al-Razi uses the kalām atomism principle: a large body seen from a distance appears small because only some parts are seen, showing additional conditions beyond the listed ones.
The argument assumes composition from indivisible parts.
3
Study al-Razi's second argument
Dust particles are visible when together but not when scattered, despite same conditions. This shows hidden conditions like density or a circularity if density is required.
4
Understand the responses
The first response denies equality of parts due to distance from the eye; the second response says the dust case is a reciprocal circularity, not temporal priority.
These responses defend the Mu'tazila limitation but are presented as weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يخفى ضعف ما ذكره بعض المعتزلة من أن العينين أعني الدنيوية والأخروية، لما كانت مثلين لزم تساويهما في الأحكام واللوازم والشروط وأن الشروط والموانع يجب أن تكون منحصرة فيما ذكرنا للدوران القطعي. ولأنه إذا قيل لنا إن هناك مرئياً آخر مقروناً بجميع ما ذكر من الشرائط، وانتفاء الموانع إلا أنه لا يرى لانتفاء شرط أو تحقق مانع غير ذلك، فنحن نقطع ببطلانه واحتج الإمام الرازي على بطلان انحصار الشرائط فيما ذكروه بوجهين : أحدهما : ومبناه على قاعدة المتكلمين أعني تركب الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ. أنا نرى الجسم الكبير من البعد صغيراً، وما ذاك إلا لرؤية بعض أجزائه دون البعض استواء الكل في الشرائط المذكورة، فأو لا اختصاص البعض بشرط وارتفاع مانع لما مع كان كذلك. وثانيهما : أنا نرى ذرات الغبار عند اجتماعها ولا نراها عند تفرقها مع حصول الشرائط المذكورة في الحالين. فعلمنا اختصاصها حالة التفرق بانتفاء شرط أو وجود مانع . لا يقال بل ذاك لانتفاء شرط الكثافة، وتحقق مانع الصغر، لأنا نقول : فحينئذ تكون رؤية كل ذرة مشروطة بانضمام الأخرى إليها وهو دور . وأجيب عن الأول بمنع التساوي فإن أجزاء الجسم متفاوتة في القرب والبعد من الحدقة . فلعل بعضاً منها يقع في حد البعد المانع من الرؤية بخلاف البعض. وعن الثاني : بأنه دور معية لا تقدم.
Then the weakness of what some Mu'tazila mentioned is not hidden: that the two eyes—I mean the worldly and the hereafter—since they are similar, they must be equal in rulings, concomitants, and conditions, and that the conditions and obstacles must be limited to what we have mentioned due to the definitive correlation. And because if it is said to us that there is another visible object accompanied by all the mentioned conditions and absence of obstacles, yet it is not seen due to the absence of a condition or the presence of another obstacle, then we would rule its falsehood definitively. Imam al-Razi argued against the limitation of conditions to what they mentioned in two ways: First, based on the rule of the mutakallimun—namely that the body is composed of indivisible parts—we see a large body from a distance as small, and that is only due to seeing some of its parts without others, despite the uniformity of all in the mentioned conditions. So there is no first/firstly? an assignment of some parts to a condition and removal of an obstacle because then it would be so. Second, we see dust particles when they are together and do not see them when they are separate, despite the mentioned conditions being present in both states. So we know that their state of separation is characterized by the absence of a condition or the presence of an obstacle. It cannot be said that this is due to the absence of the condition of density and the presence of the obstacle of smallness, because we say: then the vision of each particle would be conditioned by the joining of another to it, and this is circular. The first was answered by denying equality, for the parts of the body differ in proximity and distance from the pupil; perhaps some of them fall within the distance that prevents vision unlike others. The second was answered that it is a reciprocal circularity, not priority.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Mu'tazila position on the vision of Allah. The Mu'tazila claim that the worldly eye and the hereafter eye are similar, so conditions for vision must be identical and limited to those mentioned (facing, absence of obstacles, etc.). They argue that if someone said there is an object with all conditions yet unseen, we would definitively reject it. The author then presents Imam al-Razi's two arguments against this limitation. First argument: based on the kalām atomism, a large body seen from a distance appears small because only some of its parts are seen, even though all parts share the same conditions. This shows that there is an additional condition or obstacle (like not all parts being within the proper distance range) that is not in their list. The text 'فأو لا اختصاص البعض بشرط وارتفاع مانع لما مع كان كذلك' is somewhat unclear but likely means: 'So there is no way to assign some parts to a condition and removal of an obstacle unless that were the case' (i.e., the difference in parts indicates additional conditions). Second argument: dust particles are visible when together but not when scattered, even though conditions are the same. This shows that some hidden condition or obstacle applies. The objection that this is due to density or smallness is refuted because it would lead to circularity: each particle's visibility would depend on another. The passage then gives two responses to al-Razi's arguments: first, the parts of the body differ in distance from the eye, so some may be beyond the limit; second, the dust particle case is a reciprocal circularity, not a temporal priority. Thus the Mu'tazila claim is weakened.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that if two eyes are similar, the conditions for vision must be exactly identical and limited.
Correction: Al-Razi's examples show that other factors like distance and density affect visibility, so conditions are not limited to the ones listed by the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
What do al-Razi's two arguments demonstrate?
A) That vision is impossible for any object.
B) That the conditions for vision are limited to those listed by the Mu'tazila.
C) That the conditions for vision are not limited to facing, absence of obstacles, etc., because other factors like distance and density affect visibility.
D) That the Mu'tazila are correct about the two eyes being similar.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's arguments refute the Mu'tazila claim of limited conditions by showing that distance and density affect visibility, implying additional conditions for the vision of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi's argument about dust particles shows that your very own sight is governed by hidden conditions you cannot perceive. Does this humble your confidence that you know all the conditions for Allah's actions? Write down one area where you have insisted on your own logic over divine revelation, and commit to submitting to the Quran and Sunnah today.
?
The Mu'tazila used a definitive correlation to limit God's power to their reasoning. Where in your life do you impose limits on Allah's ability to guide, forgive, or test you based on your own assumptions? What step will you take this week to trust His wisdom over your understanding?
?
The circular response to al-Razi's second proof was dismissed as a flawed loop. Consider your own worship—are there rituals or beliefs you hold based on circular reasoning ('because my parents did it' or 'because everyone says so')? Identify one such practice and decide how to ground it in authentic evidence from revelation.

Al-Razi's Two Proofs Against Limited Conditions for Vision
Al-Razi refutes Mu'tazilite claim that conditions for vision are limited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that conditions for vision are limited, using empirical arguments based on indivisible parts and dust particles.

Core Idea
Conditions for vision are not limited; al-Razi shows extra conditions exist beyond the obvious.

Al-Razi tackles a key theological debate: are the conditions for seeing God limited to those observed in this world? The Mu'tazila argue yes, based on analogy. Al-Razi offers two clever proofs to show that even in physical vision, conditions go beyond the obvious. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the flow: initial concession, refutation, two proofs, objections, and answers.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify al-Razi's two proofs
The first proof uses the kalam principle of indivisible parts (atoms). The second uses dust particles when together vs. scattered. Note how each challenges the Mu'tazilite assumption.
3
Understand the objections and responses
After each proof, an objection is raised. For proof 1: parts differ in distance from the eye. For proof 2: the circularity is one of concomitance, not priority. Consider why these responses are significant.
The responses show the depth of the debate.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this matters: if conditions for vision are not limited, then the analogy between worldly and otherworldly vision fails, allowing for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.
This connects to the broader topic of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الرؤية عند تحقق الشرائط المذكورة في حق الشاهد، لكن لا نسلم وجوبها في الغائب عند تحقق الأمرين لجواز أن تكون الرؤيتان مختلفتين في الماهية فيختلفان في اللوازم أو تكون رؤية الخالق مشروطة بزيادة قوة إدراكية في الباصرة لا يخلقها الله تعالى إلا في الجنة في بعض الأوقات، ثم لا يخفى ضعف ما ذكره بعض المعتزلة من أن العينين أعني الدنيوية والأخروية، لما كانت مثلين لزم تساويهما في الأحكام واللوازم والشروط وأن الشروط والموانع يجب أن تكون منحصرة فيما ذكرنا للدوران القطعي. ولأنه إذا قيل لنا إن هناك مرئياً آخر مقروناً بجميع ما ذكر من الشرائط، وانتفاء الموانع إلا أنه لا يرى لانتفاء شرط أو تحقق مانع غير ذلك، فنحن نقطع ببطلانه واحتج الإمام الرازي على بطلان انحصار الشرائط فيما ذكروه بوجهين: أحدهما: ومبناه على قاعدة المتكلمين أعني تركب الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ. أنا نرى الجسم الكبير من البعد صغيراً، وما ذاك إلا لرؤية بعض أجزائه دون البعض استواء الكل في الشرائط المذكورة، فأولا اختصاص البعض بشرط وارتفاع مانع لما كان كذلك. وثانيهما: أنا نرى ذرات الغبار عند اجتماعها ولا نراها عند تفرقها مع حصول الشرائط المذكورة في الحالين. فعلمنا اختصاصها حالة التفرق بانتفاء شرط أو وجود مانع. لا يقال بل ذاك لانتفاء شرط الكثافة، وتحقق مانع الصغر، لأنا نقول: فحينئذ تكون رؤية كل ذرة مشروطة بانضمام الأخرى إليها وهو دور. وأجيب عن الأول بمنع التساوي فإن أجزاء الجسم متفاوتة في القرب والبعد من الحدقة. فلعل بعضاً منها يقع في حد البعد المانع من الرؤية بخلاف البعض. وعن الثاني: بأنه دور معية لا تقدم.
The necessity of vision when the mentioned conditions are realized is [accepted] in the case of the witnessed, but we do not grant its necessity in the unseen when the two matters are realized, because it is possible that the two visions differ in essence and thus differ in concomitants, or that the vision of the Creator is conditioned by an additional perceptive power in the eyesight which Allah does not create except in Paradise at some times. Then the weakness of what some Mu'tazila mentioned is not hidden: that the two eyes, namely the worldly and the otherworldly, since they are two similars, entail their equality in rulings, concomitants, and conditions, and that the conditions and impediments must be confined to what we mentioned due to conclusive correlation. And because if it is said to us that there is another visible object accompanied by all the mentioned conditions and the absence of impediments, yet it is not seen due to a missing condition or a present impediment other than those, then we are certain of its falsehood. And Imam al-Razi argued for the falsehood of confining the conditions to what they mentioned in two ways: The first: it is based on the principle of the theologians, namely the composition of the body from indivisible parts. We see a large body from a distance as small, and that is only due to seeing some of its parts without others, while all are equal in the mentioned conditions. So first, the specification of some parts by a condition and the absence of an impediment is because it is so. The second: we see dust particles when they are together and we do not see them when they are scattered, while the mentioned conditions are present in both states. So we know that the scattered state is specified by the absence of a condition or the presence of an impediment. It cannot be said that this is due to the absence of the condition of density and the presence of the impediment of smallness, for we say: then the vision of each particle would be conditioned by the addition of another to it, which is circular. And it was answered to the first by denying equality, for the parts of the body differ in proximity and distance from the pupil; perhaps some of them fall within the limiting distance that prevents vision unlike others. And to the second: that it is a circularity of concomitance, not of priority.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the position: the necessity of vision when conditions are met is accepted for the witnessed (shahid), but not for the unseen (gha'ib) because the two visions may differ in essence. Then it refutes a weak Mu'tazilite argument that the two eyes (worldly and otherworldly) are alike and must have the same conditions. Al-Razi provides two proofs: first, based on the kalam principle that bodies are composed of indivisible parts, we see a large body as small from a distance because we see only some parts, despite all parts having the same conditions—so there must be an additional condition. Second, we see dust particles when together but not when scattered, again indicating additional conditions. The objection that this is due to density and smallness is refuted as circular. Then both proofs are answered: the first by denying that all parts are equally distant from the eye; the second by saying the circularity is one of concomitance (daur ma'iyya) not temporal priority.

أجزاء لا تتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conditions for vision are limited to those observed in this world (e.g., distance, light).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even in physical vision, additional conditions such as atomic composition or aggregation affect visibility, so the analogy from worldly to otherworldly vision fails.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof of al-Razi against the Mu'tazilite claim that conditions for vision are limited?
A) Seeing a large body as small from a distance
B) Seeing dust particles when together but not when scattered
C) The difference between worldly and otherworldly eyes
D) The impossibility of seeing God in this world

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses empirical observations to demonstrate that conditions for vision are not limited, thereby undermining the Mu'tazilite analogy and preserving the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi points out that we see dust particles when together but not when scattered, even though the same conditions exist. In your life, are there areas where you assume that because something is not visible or obvious, it is not real? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in God's reality even when you do not perceive it, and what is one step you will take this week to strengthen that trust?
?
The Mu'tazilites limited God's ability to be seen by comparing Him to worldly objects. Are there ways you unconsciously limit God by comparing His actions to human actions? Take a moment: is there a prayer you have stopped making because you think it is impossible? Will you commit to making that du'a again, believing that God can provide conditions beyond your imagination?
?
The lesson says that vision of the Creator may require an additional perceptive power created only in Paradise. How does this affect your longing for the afterlife? Are you living in a way that eagerly awaits that enhanced vision, or are you satisfied with the sights of this world? What can you change today to set your heart on the ultimate reward of seeing your Lord?

The Fourth Doubt: The Verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'
Doubt and response on the verse denying perception of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt against the vision of Allah based on the Quranic verse 'Sight does not perceive Him,' and how the response distinguishes between 'idrak al-basar' and mere vision.

Core Idea
Denying 'idrak' does not deny vision—it denies encompassment.

The Ash‘ari theologian faces a key scriptural objection: the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is used to argue that seeing Allah is impossible. This lesson examines the doubt and the nuanced distinction between 'idrak' (perception with encompassment) and 'ru’ya' (vision) that defuses the objection. The passage also begins a second line of argument emphasizing the linguistic weight of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice the two sections: the initial doubt with response, and a re‑statement beginning with 'قوله - الرابع'.
The second section is incomplete; we only have the first of two ways mentioned.
2
Identify the doubt
The doubt asserts that 'idrak al-basar' is identical to vision or its necessary concomitant, and that the negation is general (عموم السلب), so denying vision would be a deficiency—impossible for Allah.
The doubt relies on the assumption that 'idrak' and 'ru’ya' are equivalent.
3
Understand the response
The response grants the generality but redefines 'idrak' as 'encompassing the sides of the visible object' or 'impression of the image in the eye', derived from the root meaning of 'attainment'. Thus negating 'idrak' does not negate 'ru’ya' nor imply a deficiency.
This distinction is the core theological move.
4
Notice the second argumentation
The text then calls this the strongest scriptural doubt and begins to elaborate: first, that 'idrak al-basar' is synonymous with vision by language. The passage cuts off before the second way.
The incompleteness reminds us of the high OCR risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ٤ - الشبهة الرابعة قوله تعالى: لا تدركه الأبصار. فإن إدراك البصر هو الرؤية أو لازمها وقد نفى على سبيل العموم لأن اللائق بالمقام والشائع في الاستعمال في مثل عموم السلب بإسناد النفي إلى الكل لا سلب العموم بنفي الإسناد إلى الكل ثم يبقى الكلام للتمدح بذلك فيكون نفيه نقيصة فيمتنع. قلنا لو سلم العموم في الأشخاص والأوقات فإدراك البصر رؤية على وجه إحاطة بجوانب المرئي أو انطباع الشبح في العين لما في اللفظ من معنى النيل والوصول آخذاً من أدركت فلاناً إذا لحقته فلا يلزم من نفيه نفي الرؤية ولا كونها نقصاً. قوله - الرابع: هذه هي الشبهة السمعية وأقواها قوله تعالى لا تدركه الأبصار والتمسك به من وجهين: أحدهما: أن إدراك البصر عبارة شائعة في الإدراك بالبصر إسناداً إلى الآلة، والإدراك بالبصر هو الرؤية بمعنى اتحاد المفهومين أو تلازمهما بشهادة النقل عن اللغة والتتبع لموارد الاستعمال، والقطع بامتناع إثبات أحدهما ونفي الآخر مثل: أدركت القمر ببصري وما رأيته، والجمع المعرف باللام عند عدم قرينة العهد والبعضية للعموم والاستغراق بإجماع أهل العربية والأصول وأئمة التفسير.
He said: 4 - The fourth doubt: His saying, exalted: 'Sight does not perceive Him.' For idrak al-basar is vision or its concomitant, and it has been negated in a general way, because what is appropriate to the context and common in usage in such [cases] is the generality of negation by attributing negation to the whole, not the negation of generality by negating attribution to the whole. Then the speech remains for boasting thereby, so its negation would be a deficiency, so it is impossible. We said: Even if the generality is granted regarding persons and times, idrak al-basar is vision in the manner of encompassing the sides of the visible object or the impression of the image in the eye, due to the meaning of attainment and reaching in the word, derived from 'I perceived (adraktu) so-and-so' when I caught up with him. So negating it does not necessitate negating vision nor its being a deficiency. His saying - the fourth: This is the scriptural doubt and its strongest [form] is His saying 'Sight does not perceive Him,' and it is clung to in two ways: The first: that idrak al-basar is a common expression for perception by sight, attributing to the instrument, and perception by sight is vision in the sense of the two concepts being identical or concomitant, as attested by transmission from language and examination of usage, and the certainty that it is impossible to affirm one and negate the other, like saying 'I perceived the moon with my sight and did not see it,' and the definite plural [al-abṣār] when there is no indication of covenant or partiality indicates generality and comprehensiveness by consensus of the grammarians, legal theorists, and leading exegetes.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth doubt (shubha) based on the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not perceive Him). The doubt argues that 'idrak al-basar' is vision or its concomitant, and the negation is general, making it a perfection to deny vision, so affirming vision would be an imperfection. The response: even if the negation is general, 'idrak' means encompassing or impression, not mere vision. Thus negating idrak does not negate ru'ya. Then a second section ('قوله - الرابع') states that this is the strongest scriptural doubt, and it is argued in two ways: first, that idrak al-basar is synonymous with vision by linguistic usage; second, that the definite plural 'الأبصار' indicates generality. The passage cuts off before completing the refutation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' proves that Allah cannot be seen in any sense.
Correction: The verse only denies 'idrak' which means encompassment or impression, not simple vision. Thus it does not rule out the possibility of seeing Allah without full comprehension.

Checkpoint

A) Simple vision
B) Encompassing of all sides or impression of the image
C) Perception through the intellect
D) Direct encounter without any mediation

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' does not deny vision of Allah; it denies encompassment of His essence, allowing the possibility of seeing Him without full comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The response in this lesson redefines 'idrak' from its linguistic root. When you read the Quran, do you let the precise meanings of words shape your faith, or do you often rely on surface assumptions? What might you be missing in other verses by not digging deeper?

But are you willing to accept that some of Allah's perfections are beyond human comprehension—like the perfection of being seen yet not encompassed? Is your faith comfortable with mystery, or do you demand a fully 'rational' God?

The Fourth Doubt: No Vision Can Grasp Him
Taftazani presents the fourth scriptural doubt and its refutation using linguistic principles of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth scriptural doubt against seeing Allah and its refutation through the linguistic distinction between general negation and negation of generality.

Core Idea
The negation of 'idrak' does not negate seeing; it negates a specific kind of perception.

One of the strongest scriptural objections to the possibility of seeing Allah is the Qur'anic verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103). The doubt argues that this verse universally negates vision, which would make seeing Allah a deficiency—impossible for Him. Taftazani refutes this by clarifying the meaning of 'idrak' and the linguistic structure of negation in Arabic, showing that the verse does not preclude believers from seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt presentation
Read the initial presentation of the doubt: the Qur'an negates 'idrak al-basar' in a general manner, and since that equals ru'ya or its necessary concomitant, seeing Allah is negated. But this would be a deficiency.
Notice the assumption that 'idrak' equals 'ru'ya' or its necessary concomitant.
2
Examine the first refutation
Taftazani responds by distinguishing 'idrak' as a specific kind of seeing that involves encompassing or imprinting. Negating that does not negate seeing itself, nor does it imply deficiency.
The root meaning of 'idrak' is 'attainment' or 'reaching'.
3
Understand the second presentation of the doubt
The doubt is presented again more strongly: (1) 'idrak al-basar' is synonymous with ru'ya, and (2) the definite plural 'al-abṣār' indicates generality. Thus the verse means no one can see Allah.
This is the core of the objection; the refutation must address the linguistic form.
4
Grasp the linguistic refutation
The author explains that negation with the definite plural can mean either 'negation of generality' (salb al-umum) or 'general negation' (umum al-salb), depending on the order of operations. Many Quranic examples show both uses.
This shows the verse can mean 'not everyone sees Him' rather than 'no one sees Him'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( قال : ٤ - الشبهة الرابعة قوله تعالى: لا تدركه الأبصار. فإن إدراك البصر هو الرؤية أو لازمها وقد نفى على سبيل العموم لأن اللائق بالمقام والشائع في الاستعمال في مثل عموم السلب بإسناد النفي إلى الكل لا سلب العموم بنفي الإسناد إلى الكل ثم يبقى الكلام للتمدح بذلك فيكون نفيه نقيصة فيمتنع قلنا لو سلم العموم في الأشخاص والأوقات فإدراك البصر رؤية على وجه إحاطة بجوانب المرئي أو انطباع الشبح في العين لما في اللفظ من معنى النيل والوصول آخذاً من أدركت فلاناً إذا لحقته فلا يلزم من نفيه نفي الرؤية ولا كونها نقصاً لتمتتع ) . قوله - الرابع : : هذه هي الشبهة السمعية وأقواها قوله تعالى لا تدركه الأبصار) والتمسك الابصار هو أنه لا يدركه المبصرون، وإذا كان كذلك كان قوله : وهو يدرك الأبصار المراد منه : هو يدرك المبصرون ومعتزلة البصرة يوافقوننا على أنه تعالى يبصر الأشياء فكان هو تعالى من جملة المبصرين فقوله : وهو مفاتيح الغيب جـ ٤ ص ١٦٩ ) فالله سبحانه قد أخبرنا بأنه لا يراه أحد في المستقبل، فلو رآه المؤمنون في الجنة لزم كذبه وهو محال . معنى (٤) لا يقال : إذا كان الجمع للعموم فدخول النفي عليه يفيد سلب العموم ونفي الشمول على ما هو معنى السلب الجزئي لا عموم السلب وشمول النفي على ما هو السلب الكلي فلا يكون إخباراً بأنه لا يراه أحد بل لا يراه كل أحد والأمر كذلك. لأن الكفار لا يرونه لأنا نقول كما يستعمل لسلب العموم مثل ما قام العبيد كلهم، ولم أخذ الدراهم كلها كذلك يستعمل لعموم السلب كقوله تعالى وما الله يريد ظلماً للعباد ولا تطع الكافرين والمنافقين وكذلك صريح كلمة كل مثل لا يفلح كل أحد، ولا أقبل كل درهم. ومثل والله لا يحب كل مختال فخور ولا تطع كل حلاف مهين وتحقيقه أنه إذا اعتبرت النسبة إلى الكل أولاً ثم نفيت فهو لسلب العموم ، وإن اعتبر النفي أولاً ثم نسب إلى الكل فلعموم السلب، وكذلك جميع القيود حتى إن الكلام المشتمل على نفي وقيد، قد يكون لنفي التقييد وقد يكون لتقييد النفي فمثل ما ضربته تأديباً، أي بل إهانة سلب للتعليل والعمل للفعل. وما ضربته إكراماً له أي تركت ضربه للإكرام تعليل للسلب والعمل للنفي. وما جاءني راكباً أي بل ماشياً، نفي للكيفية، وما حج مستطيعاً أي ترك الحج مع الاستطاعة تكيف للنفي، وعلى هذا الأصل ينبني أن
He said: 4 - The fourth doubt: His saying, Exalted is He: "No vision can grasp Him" (6:103). For vision's grasping (idrāk al-baṣar) is seeing (ru'ya) or its necessary concomitant, and He negated it in a general manner (ʿalā sabīl al-ʿumūm), because what befits the context and is prevalent in usage in such cases is general negation (ʿumūm al-salb) by attributing negation to the whole, not negation of generality (salab al-ʿumūm) by negating attribution to the whole; then the speech remains for boasting, so its negation would be a deficiency and thus impossible. We say: even if we grant generality in persons and times, vision's grasping is seeing in the sense of encompassing the sides of the seen or the imprint of the image in the eye, because the word contains the meaning of attainment and reaching, derived from 'I caught so-and-so' when you caught him; so its negation does not necessitate the negation of seeing, nor does it constitute a deficiency, because it is impossible. His saying - the fourth: this is the scriptural doubt and its strongest is His saying "No vision can grasp Him". It is clung to in two respects: First, that vision's grasping is a common expression for perceiving by sight, attributing to the instrument, and perceiving by sight is seeing in the sense of either conceptual identity or their necessary concomitance, as attested by transmitted language and investigation of usage, and the certainty of the impossibility of affirming one and negating the other, such as 'I perceived the moon with my sight and did not see it'. Second, the definite plural (al-jamʿ al-muʿarraf bi-l-lām) when there is no indicator of covenant or partiality indicates generality and totality (al-ʿumūm wa-l-istighrāq) by consensus of Arabic grammarians, legal theorists, and exegetes, and by the testimony of the usage of eloquent speakers and the validity of exception. (Fakhr al-Razi says: what is meant by 'sight' (al-abṣār) here is not the eyes themselves, for the eye does not perceive anything at all anywhere; rather the perceiver is the perceiver (al-mubṣir). So it must be concluded that what is meant by His saying 'He cannot be grasped by vision' is that He is not perceived by those who see. If so, then His saying 'and He perceives them' means He perceives those who see. The Basran Mu'tazila agree with us that He, exalted, sees things; thus He, exalted, is among those who see. So His saying 'and He perceives them' requires that He, exalted, is a seer of Himself. If this is the case, then He, exalted, is possibly seen in His essence, and He, exalted, sees Himself. Everyone who says that He, exalted, is possibly seen in His essence says that the believers will see Him on the Day of Resurrection. Refer to Mafātīḥ al-Ghayb vol. 4 p. 169). So God, glorified, has informed us that no one will see Him in the future; if the believers were to see Him in Paradise, then His lying would follow, which is impossible. Meaning (4): It is not said: if the plural indicates generality, then entering negation upon it yields negation of generality (salab al-ʿumūm) and denial of inclusion, which is the meaning of partial negation (al-salb al-juzʾī), not general negation (ʿumūm al-salb) and comprehensive denial, which is total negation (al-salb al-kullī). So it is not informing that no one will see Him, rather that not everyone will see Him. And the matter is thus, because the disbelievers do not see Him. For we say: just as it is used for negation of generality (salab al-ʿumūm), such as 'all the slaves did not stand' (wa mā qāma al-ʿabīdu kulluhum) and 'I did not take all the dirhams' (wa lam ākhudh al-darāhima kullahā), likewise it is used for general negation (ʿumūm al-salb), as in His saying, exalted: 'And God does not desire injustice for the servants' (40:31) and 'And obey not the disbelievers and the hypocrites' (33:48), and similarly the explicit word 'every' (kull) like 'No one prospers' (lā yufliḥu kullu aḥad) and 'I will not accept every dirham' (lā aqbilu kulla dirham), and like 'God does not love every arrogant boaster' (57:23) and 'And obey not every worthless habitual swearer' (68:10). Its verification is that if the relation to the whole is considered first and then negated, it is negation of generality (salab al-ʿumūm); if the negation is considered first and then related to the whole, it is general negation (ʿumūm al-salb). Likewise all restrictions, so that speech containing negation and a restriction may be for the negation of the restriction or for the restriction of the negation. For example, 'I did not strike him as discipline' means rather as insult, negation of the cause and act assigned to the action. 'I did not strike him as honor for him' means I left striking him as honor, a cause for the negation and act assigned to the negation. 'He did not come to me riding' means rather walking, negation of the modality. 'He did not perform the pilgrimage while able' means he left the pilgrimage despite ability, a modality for the negation. And upon this principle it is based that
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a sustained dialectical argument about the fourth doubt (الشبهة الرابعة) against the possibility of seeing Allah (رؤية الله), derived from Qur’an 6:103: “No vision can grasp Him.” The author (al-Taftazani) first states the doubt in summarized form and then provides a detailed refutation. The doubt rests on the claim that the verse negates vision of Allah universally, which would make seeing Him a deficiency for Allah, impossible. The response distinguishes ‘idrāk al-baṣar’ (vision’s grasping) from mere ru’ya (seeing), arguing that the negation is of encompassing or imprinting, not of sight itself. Then, the doubt is presented again as the strongest scriptural objection, with two supporting arguments: (1) ‘idrāk al-baṣar’ is synonymous with ru’ya, so negating it negates seeing; (2) the definite plural “al-abṣār” indicates generality. The author includes a lengthy quote from Fakhr al-Razi explaining that the verse means the perceivers do not perceive Him, not that the eyes do not see, and that this implies God sees Himself. After the quote, an argument is presented that God has informed us no one will see Him, so believing it would be a lie. Then an objection is raised: negation with the definite plural may yield negation of generality (salb al-‘umūm) rather than general negation (ʿumūm al-salb), so the verse only says not everyone sees Him, not that no one does. The author refutes this by showing that both constructions are used in Arabic, and the determining factor is the order: if you first consider the relation to the whole then negate, it is salb al-‘umūm; if you negate first then relate to the whole, it is ʿumūm al-salb. Numerous examples illustrate this. Finally, the principle is extended to all restrictions in language.

Key Terms
إدراك البصر
الرؤية
عموم السلب
سلب العموم
الجمع المعرف باللام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' absolutely negates any possibility of seeing Allah, even for believers in the afterlife.
Correction: The verse negates 'idrak' which is a specific kind of visual perception (encompassing/imprinting), not ru'ya itself. Moreover, the linguistic structure allows for interpretation as 'not everyone sees Him' (salb al-umum) rather than 'no one sees Him' (umum al-salb).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the phrase 'إدراك البصر' (vision's grasping) specifically mean?
A) Seeing in the sense of direct visual perception without any restriction.
B) Seeing that involves encompassing the sides of the seen or imprinting the image in the eye, derived from the meaning of attainment.
C) A general sense of visual awareness.
D) The act of looking.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' does not negate seeing Allah; it negates a specific kind of perception, and the linguistic structure allows for believers to see Him in Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that a single verse can be misunderstood due to a lack of linguistic precision. When you read the Qur'an, do you ever assume you know the exact meaning of a verse without checking the original Arabic or scholarly commentary? What might you be missing in your current understanding of Allah's attributes? Will you commit to studying one verse a day with a tafsir to deepen your 'aqidah?
?
The objector in this passage built an entire argument on a surface-level reading, missing the deeper linguistic nuance. In your own life, have you ever settled for a simplistic understanding of Allah that left you with a distant or inaccurate picture of Him? How might embracing the precise meanings of divine attributes change the way you relate to Allah in prayer and daily life?
?
The distinction between 'general negation' and 'negation of generality' shows that the order of thought matters. When you reflect on Allah's transcendence, do you ever fall into the trap of negating His immanence? How can you balance affirming Allah's nearness while preserving His majesty, based on the precise wording of revelation? What practical step can you take this week to correct a wrong assumption you hold about Allah?

The Second Argument and Its First Refutation
Sanusi presents the opponent's argument and gives the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second argument that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise implying vision is a defect, and the author's first refutation limiting the negation to this world and addressing the objection about eternal praise.

Core Idea
Praise does not imply eternal negation of actions, only of attributes.

The opponent argues that the verse is a praise of God, so its opposite (vision) would be a defect, which is impossible for God, thus vision is impossible. But this argument assumes the negation applies to all times and states. In this lesson, we examine the opponent's reasoning and the author's first answer, which restricts the negation to this world and defends the possibility of vision in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent presents his reasoning from the verse. Notice how he claims that because the negation comes in the context of praise, its opposite must be a defect.
The opponent's logic: praise → opposite is defect → impossible for God → vision is impossible.
2
Study the first answer
Read the author's first answer: even if we grant universal negation, we do not grant it for all times and states. The verse is interpreted as negation in this world only.
This is a common method: reconciling conflicting evidences by restricting the scope.
3
Examine the objection and reply
An objector says praise endures eternally, so negation must also be eternal. The author replies that eternity pertains to essence and attributes, not actions. Vision is an action, which can be created or not.
This reply distinguishes between what is essential and what is accidental.
4
Understand the final point on practical vs theoretical
Even if we grant generality of times, the argument only yields probability, which is sufficient for practical matters but not for theoretical beliefs about God.
This shows that speculative theological arguments require certainty, not mere probability.

وثانيهما : أي ثاني وجهي التمسك بالآية أن نفي إدراكه بالبصر وارد مورد التمدح مدرج في أثناء المدح، فيكون نقيضه وهو الإدراك بالبصر نقصاً، وهو على الله تعالى محال فيدل هذا الوجه على نفي الجواز.

والجواب : أولاً : أنه لو سلم عموم الابصار وكون الكلام لعموم السلب - لكن لا نسلم عمومه في الأحوال والأوقات، فيحمل على نفي الرؤية في الدنيا جمعاً بين الأدلة .
وأورد عليه أولاً أن هذا تمدح ، وما به التمدح يدوم في الدنيا والآخرة ولا يزول. ودفع بأن امتناع الزوال إنما هو فيما يرجع إلى الذات والصفات، وأما ما يرجع إلى الأفعال فقد يزول لحدوثها. والرؤية من هذا القبيل فقد يخلقها الله تعالى في العين وقد لا يخلق ثم .
ولو سلم عموم الأوقات فغايته الظهور والرجحان، ومثله إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون العلميات .

وثانياً: أنا لا نسلم أن الإدراك بالبصر هو الرؤية أو لازم لها بل هو رؤية مخصوصة. وهو أن يكون على وجه الإحاطة بجوانب المرئي إذ حقيقته النيل والوصول مأخوذاً من أدركت فلاناً إذا لحقته، ولهذا يصح رأيت القمر وما أدركه بصري لإحاطة الغيم به ، ولا يصح أدركه البصر وما رأيته فيكون أخص من الرؤية ملزوماً لها بمنزلة الإحاطة من العلم ، فلا يلزم من نفيه نفيها، ولا من كون نفيه مدحاً كون الرؤية نقصاً، واستدلالهم بأن قولنا أدركت القمر ببصري وما رأيته تناقض ، إنما يفيد ما ذكرنا لا ما ذكروا ، ونقلهم عن أئمة اللغة افتراء، فإن إدراك الحواس مستعار أدركت فلاناً إذا لحقته، وقد صار حقيقة عرفية، فالرجوع فيه إلى العرف دون اللغة.
And the second of them: that is, the second of the two ways of adhering to the verse: that the negation of His perception by sight occurs in the context of praise, inserted within praise; so its opposite, which is perception by sight, would be a defect, and that is impossible with respect to Allah Almighty; so this way indicates the negation of permissibility.

The answer: First: Even if the generality of sights and that the speech is for universal negation is granted, we do not grant its generality in states and times; so it is interpreted as negation of vision in this world, to reconcile the evidences.
And it was objected to this first [point] that this is praise, and that by which praise is attained endures in this world and the Hereafter and does not cease. It was repelled: the impossibility of cessation is only with respect to what pertains to essence and attributes; as for what pertains to actions, it may cease due to their temporality. And vision is of this kind: Allah may create it in the eye or not create it.
And even if the generality of times is granted, its ultimate is appearance and preponderance, and such is only considered in practical matters, not in theoretical ones.

Second: We do not grant that perception by sight is vision or a necessary concomitant of it; rather, it is a specific kind of vision. It is that it be in the manner of encompassing the sides of the visible object, since its real meaning is attainment and reaching, derived from 'I caught up with so-and-so' when I reached him. For this reason, it is correct to say 'I saw the moon but my sight did not perceive it' due to the cloud encompassing it, and it is not correct to say 'my sight perceived it but I did not see it'. So it is more specific than vision, concomitant to it, like encompassing is to knowledge. Thus, from its negation, the negation of vision does not necessarily follow, nor from its being praised does vision become a defect. Their argument that our saying 'I perceived the moon with my sight and I did not see it' is a contradiction only proves what we have said, not what they claimed. And their quoting from linguistic authorities is a fabrication. Indeed, the perception of the senses is metaphorical, derived from 'I caught up with so-and-so' when I reached him, and it has become a conventional reality; so reference in it is to custom, not to language.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The opponent argues that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise of God, so its opposite (vision) would be a defect, which is impossible for God, thus vision is impossible. The author gives two answers. First: Even if we accept the universal negation, we do not accept it for all times and states; it refers only to this world, allowing vision in the Hereafter. Then an objection is raised: praise endures eternally, so the negation must also be eternal. The author replies: eternal duration pertains only to essence and attributes; vision is an action, which may be created or not. Even if we grant generality of times, it only gives probability, which suffices for practical matters (like actions) but not for theoretical matters (like beliefs). Second: The term 'إدراك' (perception) is not synonymous with 'رؤية' (vision). 'إدراك' means comprehensive perception, encompassing the object, which is impossible for God, but simple vision (رؤية) is possible. The author gives linguistic evidence: one can say 'I saw the moon but did not perceive it' if it is obscured. Thus, negating 'إدراك' does not negate 'رؤية'. The opponent's claim of contradiction is refuted, and their reliance on linguistic authorities is dismissed as fabrication; the term 'إدراك' is a metaphorical usage that has become conventional, so its meaning is determined by custom, not lexicography.

Key Terms
التمسك بالآية
التمدح
نفي الجواز
العمليات
العلميات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That praise of God for negating something implies that the opposite is a defect for all times and states.
Correction: Praise can be for a temporary negation; it does not require eternal negation, especially for actions that may be created later.

Checkpoint
According to the author's first answer, why does the negation in the verse not apply to the Hereafter?
A) Because the verse explicitly mentions 'this world'
B) Because the verse is general but we restrict it to this world to reconcile evidences
C) Because the opponent's argument is invalid
D) Because vision in the Hereafter is impossible

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that the argument for impossibility fails because the negation in the verse is restricted to this world, and vision in the Hereafter remains possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a single verse completely settles a matter about Allah, only to later realize you missed its context? How does this lesson challenge you to be more humble in interpreting the Quran?
?
Think of a time when you treated a temporary weakness or sin as if it were a permanent flaw in your character. How does the distinction between essence/attributes and actions here reframe how you see yourself—and how Allah sees you?
?
This lesson shows that even praise can be misinterpreted. Where in your life are you relying on what feels ‘praiseworthy’ in your own eyes rather than submitting to the full revelation? What would it cost you to let go of that assumption?

The Second Refutation: Perception vs Vision
Sanusi gives the second refutation by distinguishing 'idrak' from 'ru'ya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's second refutation: that 'idrak' (perception) is not the same as 'ru'ya' (vision) but is a specific kind involving comprehensive encompassing, so negating 'idrak' does not negate 'ru'ya'.

Core Idea
Idrak is a specific kind of vision, not vision itself.

The opponent's argument assumes that 'idrak' (perception) is identical to 'ru'ya' (vision). The author's second answer dismantles this assumption by showing that 'idrak' means comprehensive perception, encompassing the object from all sides, while 'ru'ya' is simpler. The author provides linguistic evidence and argues that the opponent's reliance on language authorities is unfounded; the term has become conventional, so custom determines its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the passage starting with 'وثانياً' where the author states his second point. Note the claim: 'idrak' is not vision or its necessary concomitant, but a specific kind of vision.
This is a semantic distinction aimed at refuting the opponent's inference.
2
Understand the definition of 'idrak'
The author defines 'idrak' as perception that encompasses the object, derived from the root meaning 'to catch up with'. It is more specific than simple vision.
The linguistic root indicates attaining and encompassing.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The author gives an example: one can say 'I saw the moon but my sight did not perceive it' when it is obscured. This shows that 'idrak' and 'ru'ya' are not interchangeable.
The example proves that negation of 'idrak' does not entail negation of 'ru'ya'.
4
Refute the opponent's linguistic claim
The opponent claimed that saying 'I perceived it with my sight but did not see it' is contradictory. The author says this only proves his own point, not the opponent's. Moreover, the opponent's citation of language authorities is a fabrication; 'idrak' has become a conventional term.
The final point emphasizes that the meaning is determined by custom (urf), not pure lexicography.
