Human Action and Divine Power: The Ash'ari Doctrine of Kasb
Taftazani presents the Ash'ari position on human action and kasb, refuting jabr and defining kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that human actions occur by God's power while the servant acquires them through kasb, and how this refutes both Mu'tazilite and jabr extremes.

Core Idea
The servant's action is from God but the servant acquires it: kasb is real but not causal.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid opens the discussion on human action and divine power. Taftazani states the Ash'ari doctrine: the servant's action is created by God, and the servant only has acquisition (kasb). He then reviews rival positions, refutes the extreme of jabr, and provides the true definition of kasb as an additional relational matter. Understanding this distinction is essential for avoiding extremes in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style and the range of views he mentions.
The passage contains both doctrine and refutation.
2
Identify the different schools
The passage lists the Mu'tazila, philosophers, al-Ustadh, and al-Qadi. Note their respective positions on the role of human power in action.
These are the main theological opponents of the Ash'ari view.
3
Understand the refutation of jabr
The author states that the jabr position (no effect for human power) is self-evidently false. Ask yourself why it is considered so obvious.
This shows the Ash'aris reject both extreme determinism and human autonomy.
4
Analyze the definitions of kasb
The passage gives several definitions of kasb. Notice that the author favors the view that kasb is an 'additional matter' (amr iḍāfī) that does not cause the act but describes the agent's relation to it.
This is the key technical concept of Ash'ari theology.
5
Reflect on the theological balance
Consider how this doctrine maintains both God's omnipotence and human responsibility. Write down your own understanding in one sentence.
This is the core insight of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في أفعاله وفيه مباحث (۱) .
المبحث الأول : فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، وإنما للعبد الكسب . والمعتزلة : بقدرة العبد صحة، والحكماء إيجاباً، والأستاذ بهما على أن يتعلقا جميعاً به، والقاضي على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بأصله وقدرة العبد بوضعه ككونه طاعة أو معصية، وأما الجبر أنه لا أثر لقدرة العبد أصلاً لا إيجاداً ولا كسباً فضروري البطلان، والكسب قبل ذلك الوصف الذي به تتعلق قدرة العبد وقيل الفعل المخلوق بقدرة الله من حيث خلق للعبد قدرة متعلقة به وقيل ما يقع به المقدور بلا صحة انفراد القادر. وما يقع في محل القدرة، والحق أنه ظاهر والخفاء في التعبير، والأوضح أنه أمر إضافي يجب من العبد، ولا يوجب وجود المقدور، بل اتصاف الفاعل بالمقدور كتعيين أحد الطرفين ،وترجيحه، وصرف القدرة .
(۱) راجع ما كتبه ابن حزم في هذا الموضوع فهو في غاية الوجاهة ورده على عباد بن سليمان تلميذ هشام بن عمرو الغوطي الذي قال: إن الله تعالى لم يخلق الكفار لأنهم ناس وكفر معاً لكن خلق أجسامهم دون كفرهم جـ ٣ ص ٥٤ وما بعدها .
The fifth chapter on His actions, and it contains investigations (1). The first investigation: The action of the servant occurs by the power of God Most High, and the servant only has acquisition (kasb). The Mu'tazila: it occurs by the power of the servant with correctness, and the philosophers necessarily, and al-Ustadh (says) that both (powers) are to be attached to it together, and al-Qadi (says) that God's power attaches to its essence and the servant's power to its description (waḍ‘) such as its being obedience or disobedience. As for jabr (compulsion), that there is no effect for the servant's power at all, neither in bringing into existence nor in acquisition, it is self-evidently false. And kasb before that is the attribute by which the servant's power attaches to the act, and it is said: the act created by God's power from the aspect that He created for the servant a power attached to it, and it is said: that by which the determined act occurs without the soundness of the independent agent, and what occurs in the locus of power. The truth is that it is apparent and the obscurity is in expression, and the clearest (expression) is that it is an additional matter (amr iḍāfī) that is necessitated from the servant, and does not necessitate the existence of the determined act, but rather the description of the agent with the determined act, like specifying one of the two alternatives, and giving it preponderance (tarjīḥ), and directing one's power. (1) Refer to what Ibn Ḥazm wrote on this subject, for it is most excellent, and his refutation of ‘Abbād ibn Sulaymān, the student of Hishām ibn ‘Amr al-Ghūṭī, who said: God Most High did not create the disbelievers because they are people and disbelief together, but He created their bodies without their disbelief, vol. 3, p. 54 and after.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fifth chapter on God's actions, and it contains several investigations. The first investigation concerns human action and divine power. The author, al-Taftazani (or the original matn writer), states the Ash'ari position: human action occurs by God's power, and the human has only acquisition (kasb). Then he lists other views: the Mu'tazila hold that the act occurs by the human's power with soundness (i.e., the human is the true agent); the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') hold it occurs necessarily (by the human's power, implying causal necessity); al-Ustadh (probably Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini) holds that both powers (God's and the human's) are attached to the act simultaneously; al-Qadi (likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar from the Mu'tazila) says God's power attaches to the act's essence and the servant's power attaches to its descriptive aspect, such as being obedience or disobedience. The author then refutes the extreme jabr position that human power has no effect at all—this is self-evidently false. After this, he turns to the definition of kasb. He gives several definitions: (1) the attribute by which the human's power attaches to the act, (2) the act created by God's power from the aspect that He created for the human a power attached to it, (3) that by which the act occurs without the soundness of the independent agent, (4) what occurs in the locus of power. Then he states the true opinion (al-ḥaqq): kasb is apparent in meaning, but the obscurity is in the expression. The clearest expression is that kasb is an additional matter (amr iḍāfī) that is necessitated from the human but does not cause the act to exist; rather, it is the description of the agent with the act, similar to specifying one of two alternatives and giving it preponderance and directing one's power. The footnote references Ibn Hazm's discussion and his refutation of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman, who held that God did not create the disbelief of disbelievers.

Correction: The passage clarifies that kasb is an attributive matter, not creative; it is a real relation but not causal.

Checkpoint

A) The act created by God's power for which the human has no effect.
B) An additional matter required from the servant that does not necessitate the existence of the act but describes the agent with the act.
C) The human's power alone produces the act with soundness.
D) Both God's and the human's powers necessarily together produce the act.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb affirms that human actions are created by God but the human acquires them through a non-causal attribution, preserving divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a difficult task, do you quietly credit your own effort and talent, or do you genuinely feel that God created that action and you merely acquired it? What would it look like to live out the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb in your daily routine?
?
Have you ever felt despair because you believed your actions were entirely up to you (like the Mu'tazila) or entirely irrelevant (like the Jabrites)? How does the middle path of kasb change the way you pray for help before an exam or a presentation?
?

In your own life, what is one area where you pretend God chooses for you, but deep down you know you are merely specifying and preferring? Will you start taking ownership of your choices without denying that God creates the outcome?

Divine Knowledge Necessitates Events
Establishing divine knowledge necessitates events, eliminating true choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's knowledge of all particulars renders every known future act either necessary or impossible, leaving no genuine alternative for the servant.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cancels contingency: what He knows must occur cannot fail.

The author begins by establishing a foundational premise: God knows all things, past and future, perfectly. From this, he draws a stark conclusion: whatever God knows will occur must occur, and whatever He knows will not occur is impossible. This is not because the acts are impossible in themselves, but because divine knowledge infallibly attaches to its object. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its immediate implication for human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how the author moves from a general truth about God's knowledge to a specific conclusion about human acts.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides known future events into two categories: those God knows will occur (necessary) and those He knows will not occur (impossible). Find the phrases that mark this division.
These are 'يجب وقوعه' and 'يمتنع وقوعه'.
3
Trace the implication for human capacity
The author concludes that nothing remains in the servant's capacity in the sense of genuine choice. Ask yourself: why does divine knowledge remove genuine alternatives?
Think about what it means for an act to be known infallibly.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'possible in itself'
The passage notes that the act may be 'possible in itself' (mumkin bi-dhātihi) even while being necessary or impossible by relation to God's knowledge. Distinguish these two perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد ثبت أن الله تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ما كان وما سيكون، وأنه يستحيل عليه الجهل وكل ما علم الله أنه يقع يجب وقوعه، وكل ما علم الله أنه لا يقع يمتنع وقوعه، نظراً إلى تعلق العلم، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى ذاته، ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع باقياً في مكنة العبد بمعنى أنه إن شاء فعله وإن شاء تركه. فإن قيل : يجوز أن يعلم الله تعالى أن فعل العبد يقع بقدرته واختياره، فلا يكون خارجاً عن مكنته . من[؟] قلنا : فيجب أن يقع البتة بقدرته واختياره بحيث لا يتمكن من اختيار الترك، وهذا هو المراد بالانتهاء إلى الاضطرار. غاية الأمر أن يكون بإيجاده لكن لا على وجه الاستقلال والاختيار التام كما هو مذهب المعتزلة. وقد أشرنا إلى أن القصد بعض الأدلة إلى الالزام دون الإتمام نعم يرد نقض الدليل بفعل الباري تعالى الجريانه فيه مع الاتفاق على كونه بقدرته واختياره ويمكن دفعه بأن الاختياري ما يكون الفاعل متمكناً من تركه عند إرادة فعله لا بعده، وهذا متحقق في فعل الباري، لأن إرادته قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأنه يقع في وقته وجائز أن يتعلق حينئذ بتركه، وليس حينئذ سابقة علم ليتحقق الوجوب، أو الامتناع إذ لا قبل للأزل، فالحاصل أن تعلق العلم والإرادة معاً فلا محذور بخلاف إرادة العبد، وتقرير الإمام في المطالب أنه لما وجب في الأزل وقوع الفعل أو لا وقوعه في وقته لزم أن يكون لهذا الوجوب سبب ، وليس من العبد لأن الحادث لا يصلح سبباً للأزلي . بل من الله تعالى . وليس هو العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم، لا مستتبع بل القدرة والإرادة، إذ بهما التأثير فثبت أن المؤثر في فعل العبد قدرة الله تعالى إما ابتداء أو بوسط وهو المطلوب، وهذا ضعيف جداً، لكن النقض مندفع عنه.
It has been established that God, exalted, knows particulars, what was and what will be, and that ignorance is impossible for Him. Everything that God knows will occur, its occurrence is necessary; everything that God knows will not occur, its occurrence is impossible, in consideration of the attachment of knowledge, even if it is possible in itself and with respect to its essence. And nothing of the necessary and impossible remains within the capacity of the servant in the sense that if he wills he does it and if he wills he leaves it. If it is said: It is permissible that God, exalted, knows that the servant's act occurs by his power and choice, so it is not outside his capacity. We say: Then it must occur absolutely by his power and choice such that he is not able to choose leaving it, and this is what is meant by ending in compulsion. The upshot is that it is by His bringing into existence but not in the manner of independence and complete choice as is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila. And we have indicated that the intent of some proofs is to compel rather than to complete. Yes, a counterexample is raised against the proof by the act of God, exalted, because it runs in it despite agreement that it is by His power and choice. It can be repelled by saying: The voluntary is that whose doer is able to leave it at the time of willing the act, not after it. This is realized in the act of God because His will is eternal, attached in pre-eternity to its occurrence at its time, and it is possible that it then be attached to leaving it, and there is no prior knowledge to necessitate obligation or impossibility since there is no before to pre-eternity. So the conclusion is that the attachment of knowledge and will together has no harm, unlike the will of the servant. And the exposition of the Imam in al-Matalib: When in pre-eternity the occurrence or non-occurrence of the act at its time became necessary, it follows that this necessity has a cause, and it is not from the servant because the temporal is not suitable as a cause for the eternal. Rather from God, exalted. And it is not knowledge because it follows the known, not necessitates; rather it is power and will, for effect is by them. So it is established that the effective agent in the servant's act is God's power, either directly or through an intermediary, which is what is sought. And this is very weak, but the counterexample is repelled by it.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from divine knowledge for the necessitation of human actions, followed by an objection and answer, and a critique of an Imām al-Rāzī's argument. The author begins by stating as established that God knows all particulars, past and future. Since ignorance is impossible for God, everything He knows will occur must occur, and everything He knows will not occur cannot occur. This is due to the very nature of knowledge (`ilm) as attached to its object. Although the act may be possible in itself, once known by God it becomes either necessary (wājib) or impossible (mumtani`), leaving nothing in the servant's capacity that he can genuinely choose. The objector then raises a concern: it is possible that God knows the act will occur by the servant's own power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), so the act remains within the servant's capacity. The author answers: indeed, the act occurs by the servant's power and choice, but in such a way that he cannot choose to leave it—this is precisely what is meant by being driven to compulsion (iḍṭirār). The difference from the Muʿtazila is that the act is produced (ījād) by the servant but not independently or with full choice. The author then notes that some proofs aim only to compel the opponent, not to give a full demonstration. A counterexample is raised: God's own acts are by His power and choice, yet they are also known; so why are they not necessitated? The defense: a voluntary act (ikhtiyārī) is defined as that which the agent can leave at the moment of willing the act, not after it. In God's case, His will is eternal and was attached to the act at its time, but before that attachment (in pre-eternity) there was no prior knowledge necessitating it, so no compulsion. Finally, the author summarizes al-Rāzī's argument from al-Maṭālib: since in pre-eternity the act's occurrence or non-occurrence was necessary, it must have a cause—not from the servant (since temporal cannot cause eternal), but from God. That cause is not knowledge (which follows the known) but power and will. The conclusion is that God is the real agent in the servant's act, directly or indirectly. The author calls this argument very weak, but says the counterexample is still repelled by it.

واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows an act will occur by the servant's power, the servant has full free will.
Correction: The passage shows that divine knowledge removes genuine alternative possibilities because the act's occurrence becomes necessary relative to that knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is an act that God knows will occur considered necessary?
A) Because it is impossible in itself.
B) Because of the attachment of divine knowledge to its occurrence.
C) Because the servant's will is coerced.
D) Because it is predetermined by external causes.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of a future event renders its occurrence necessary relative to that knowledge, even if the event is possible in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your past sins that God knew would happen before they occurred, do you feel trapped by fate or liberated by mercy? The passage says those sins were necessary relative to God's knowledge—does that reduce your personal responsibility in your own eyes? How will you respond to the next temptation knowing that God already knows whether you will fall?
?
You plan your future, but this passage claims that whatever God knows will happen must happen. Does that make your planning feel meaningless? Are you truly trusting God's decree while still striving, or do you secretly believe your choices are the ultimate deciders? Examine your heart today: in what area of life do you resist submitting to God's knowledge because you want to believe you could have done otherwise?
?
If nothing that is necessary or impossible remains under your genuine choice, then every good deed you do was made necessary by God's knowledge. Does that fill you with humility or with a sense of entitlement? When you read this passage, do you feel a weight lifted (I don't have to carry the burden of ultimate control) or a sting (my obedience is not truly my own)? What change in your daily worship does this truth demand?

Objection and Answer on Human Capacity
Responding to objection, defining compulsion, and addressing a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine knowledge can be of an act done by the servant's own choice, the author's answer defining compulsion, the counterexample from God's own acts, and the critique of al-Razi's argument.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge compels the servant's act to occur by his power but without ability to omit.

The previous conclusion that nothing remains in the servant's capacity invites an obvious objection: God could know that an act occurs by the servant's own power and choice, so why would that remove the servant's capacity? The author answers with a nuanced definition of compulsion (iḍṭirār). He then addresses a counterexample concerning God's own acts and analyzes a proof from Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objector says divine knowledge does not preclude the servant's capacity because God knows the act will occur by the servant's power and choice. The answer redefines 'remaining in capacity' as having the ability to choose both alternatives.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الانتهاء إلى الاضطرار'.
2
Understand the definition of compulsion
The author agrees the act occurs by the servant's power and choice but in a way that he cannot choose the opposite. This is compulsion (iḍṭirār). Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view.
3
Examine the counterexample
A counterexample is raised: God's own acts are by His power and choice, yet they are also known. The defense defines 'voluntary' as being able to leave the act at the moment of willing, not after.
The defense hinges on the eternal nature of God's will.
4
Review al-Razi's argument and critique
The author summarizes al-Razi's proof in al-Matalib that a necessitating cause is needed from God (power and will) and critiques it as very weak, but claims it repels the counterexample.
Consider why the author calls al-Razi's argument weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد ثبت أن الله تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ما كان وما سيكون، وأنه يستحيل عليه الجهل وكل ما علم الله أنه يقع يجب وقوعه، وكل ما علم الله أنه لا يقع يمتنع وقوعه، نظراً إلى تعلق العلم، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى ذاته، ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع باقياً في مكنة العبد بمعنى أنه إن شاء فعله وإن شاء تركه. فإن قيل : يجوز أن يعلم الله تعالى أن فعل العبد يقع بقدرته واختياره، فلا يكون خارجاً عن مكنته . من[؟] قلنا : فيجب أن يقع البتة بقدرته واختياره بحيث لا يتمكن من اختيار الترك، وهذا هو المراد بالانتهاء إلى الاضطرار. غاية الأمر أن يكون بإيجاده لكن لا على وجه الاستقلال والاختيار التام كما هو مذهب المعتزلة. وقد أشرنا إلى أن القصد بعض الأدلة إلى الالزام دون الإتمام نعم يرد نقض الدليل بفعل الباري تعالى الجريانه فيه مع الاتفاق على كونه بقدرته واختياره ويمكن دفعه بأن الاختياري ما يكون الفاعل متمكناً من تركه عند إرادة فعله لا بعده، وهذا متحقق في فعل الباري، لأن إرادته قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأنه يقع في وقته وجائز أن يتعلق حينئذ بتركه، وليس حينئذ سابقة علم ليتحقق الوجوب، أو الامتناع إذ لا قبل للأزل، فالحاصل أن تعلق العلم والإرادة معاً فلا محذور بخلاف إرادة العبد، وتقرير الإمام في المطالب أنه لما وجب في الأزل وقوع الفعل أو لا وقوعه في وقته لزم أن يكون لهذا الوجوب سبب ، وليس من العبد لأن الحادث لا يصلح سبباً للأزلي . بل من الله تعالى . وليس هو العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم، لا مستتبع بل القدرة والإرادة، إذ بهما التأثير فثبت أن المؤثر في فعل العبد قدرة الله تعالى إما ابتداء أو بوسط وهو المطلوب، وهذا ضعيف جداً، لكن النقض مندفع عنه.
It has been established that God, exalted, knows particulars, what was and what will be, and that ignorance is impossible for Him. Everything that God knows will occur, its occurrence is necessary; everything that God knows will not occur, its occurrence is impossible, in consideration of the attachment of knowledge, even if it is possible in itself and with respect to its essence. And nothing of the necessary and impossible remains within the capacity of the servant in the sense that if he wills he does it and if he wills he leaves it. If it is said: It is permissible that God, exalted, knows that the servant's act occurs by his power and choice, so it is not outside his capacity. We say: Then it must occur absolutely by his power and choice such that he is not able to choose leaving it, and this is what is meant by ending in compulsion. The upshot is that it is by His bringing into existence but not in the manner of independence and complete choice as is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila. And we have indicated that the intent of some proofs is to compel rather than to complete. Yes, a counterexample is raised against the proof by the act of God, exalted, because it runs in it despite agreement that it is by His power and choice. It can be repelled by saying: The voluntary is that whose doer is able to leave it at the time of willing the act, not after it. This is realized in the act of God because His will is eternal, attached in pre-eternity to its occurrence at its time, and it is possible that it then be attached to leaving it, and there is no prior knowledge to necessitate obligation or impossibility since there is no before to pre-eternity. So the conclusion is that the attachment of knowledge and will together has no harm, unlike the will of the servant. And the exposition of the Imam in al-Matalib: When in pre-eternity the occurrence or non-occurrence of the act at its time became necessary, it follows that this necessity has a cause, and it is not from the servant because the temporal is not suitable as a cause for the eternal. Rather from God, exalted. And it is not knowledge because it follows the known, not necessitates; rather it is power and will, for effect is by them. So it is established that the effective agent in the servant's act is God's power, either directly or through an intermediary, which is what is sought. And this is very weak, but the counterexample is repelled by it.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from divine knowledge for the necessitation of human actions, followed by an objection and answer, and a critique of an Imām al-Rāzī's argument. The author begins by stating as established that God knows all particulars, past and future. Since ignorance is impossible for God, everything He knows will occur must occur, and everything He knows will not occur cannot occur. This is due to the very nature of knowledge (`ilm) as attached to its object. Although the act may be possible in itself, once known by God it becomes either necessary (wājib) or impossible (mumtani`), leaving nothing in the servant's capacity that he can genuinely choose. The objector then raises a concern: it is possible that God knows the act will occur by the servant's own power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), so the act remains within the servant's capacity. The author answers: indeed, the act occurs by the servant's power and choice, but in such a way that he cannot choose to leave it—this is precisely what is meant by being driven to compulsion (iḍṭirār). The difference from the Muʿtazila is that the act is produced (ījād) by the servant but not independently or with full choice. The author then notes that some proofs aim only to compel the opponent, not to give a full demonstration. A counterexample is raised: God's own acts are by His power and choice, yet they are also known; so why are they not necessitated? The defense: a voluntary act (ikhtiyārī) is defined as that which the agent can leave at the moment of willing the act, not after it. In God's case, His will is eternal and was attached to the act at its time, but before that attachment (in pre-eternity) there was no prior knowledge necessitating it, so no compulsion. Finally, the author summarizes al-Rāzī's argument from al-Maṭālib: since in pre-eternity the act's occurrence or non-occurrence was necessary, it must have a cause—not from the servant (since temporal cannot cause eternal), but from God. That cause is not knowledge (which follows the known) but power and will. The conclusion is that God is the real agent in the servant's act, directly or indirectly. The author calls this argument very weak, but says the counterexample is still repelled by it.

Key Terms
اضطرار
إرادة
قدرة
اختيار
قديم
حادث
مطالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an act is by the servant's own power and choice, it must be fully free.
Correction: The passage explains that an act can be by the servant's power and choice yet still be compelled, because he cannot choose the opposite.

Checkpoint
How does the author define 'voluntary' (ikhtiyārī) in the defense against the counterexample?
A) An act that the agent can do or not do at will.
B) An act whose doer can leave it at the time of willing, not after.
C) An act produced by free choice without any external influence.
D) An act that is not known beforehand.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge compels human acts in a way that preserves the servant's power and choice but removes the ability to choose the opposite, while God's own acts remain voluntary due to the nature of His eternal will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own decisions, do you ever sense that despite your freedom, there is a deeper necessity at work? How does this lesson's distinction between power and ability to omit challenge or affirm your experience?
?
If your every act is ultimately compelled by divine knowledge, how does that affect your sense of moral responsibility? Do you find that thought comforting or unsettling, and why?
?
The passage argues that God's acts are voluntary because His will is eternal, while human acts are not. How does this difference shape your understanding of God's justice when you reflect on your own sins and shortcomings?

The Argument from Divine Will and Its Refutation
Presenting and refuting the argument from divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from divine will that seeks to negate human free will and its two refutations within the Ash'arite framework.

Core Idea
God's will does not necessitate human action; the argument's exhaustive division is false.

This passage presents a theological argument that attempts to use divine will to prove determinism, and then refutes it from two angles. It parallels a similar argument from divine knowledge. The refutation hinges on denying that God must will either the act or its omission, and denying that His will forces the act to occur from the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get a sense of the argument and its refutation.
2
Identify the structure
Notice the argument (if God wills occurrence, necessary; if non-occurrence, impossible) and then two 'رد' refutations.
The refutations are introduced by 'أولاً' and 'ثانياً'.
3
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation denies the exhaustive division (ḥaṣr) — it is possible that God does not will either the act or its omission.
This undercuts the premise that God's will must attach to one of the two sides.
4
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation denies that God's will necessitates occurrence from the servant, citing Ash'arite doctrine (acquisition).
Even if God wills something, it does not force the human act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما التمسك) بأن مراده تعالى إما الوقوع أو اللاوقوع، فرد بتجويز أن لا يريد أحدهما وأن يقع خلاف مراده. كما استدل على وجوب الفعل أو الترك بتعلق العلم، فكذا يتعلق الإرادة وتقريره أن فعل العبد ما أن يريد الله تعالى وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع ، فلا يكون باختيار العبد ، ورد أولاً بمنع الحصر لجواز أن لا تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بشيء من طرفي الفعل والترك . وثانياً: بمنع وجوب وقوع ما أراده الله تعالى من العبد على ما هو المذهب عندهم كما سيجيء.
He said: (And as for the attachment) that His will, exalted, is either occurrence or non-occurrence, it was refuted by allowing that He may not will either one and that what is contrary to His will may occur. Just as an argument was made for the necessity of the act or omission by the attachment of knowledge, so likewise the will attaches. Its exposition: The servant's act—if God, exalted, wills its occurrence, it is necessary; if He wills its non-occurrence, it is impossible; thus it is not by the servant's choice. It was refuted first by denying the exhaustive division, because it is permissible that God's will does not attach to either side of act and omission. Second, by denying the necessity of the occurrence of what God, exalted, wills from the servant, according to their doctrine, as will come.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a parallel argument to the one from knowledge, but now based on divine will (irāda). The argument states that God's will is either for the occurrence or non-occurrence of an act. If He wills occurrence, it must occur; if He wills non-occurrence, it is impossible. Thus the act is not by the servant's choice. The refutation is twofold. First, the exhaustive division (ḥaṣr) is denied: it is possible that God's will is not attached to either side; He may not will the act or its omission. Second, even if He wills something, it does not necessarily follow that it must occur from the servant, because according to the opponents' own doctrine (the Ashʿarites, as will be explained later), God's will does not necessitate the act's occurrence; rather, the act is created by God and the servant merely acquires it. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
إرادة
وقوع
لاوقوع
حصر
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills something, it must always happen from the one willed, negating human choice.
Correction: In Ash'arite theology, God's will does not necessitate the act from the servant; the act is created by God and acquired by the servant, preserving choice.

Checkpoint
What are the two refutations of the argument from divine will given in the passage?
A) Denying God's will and denying His power.
B) Denying the exhaustive division and denying the necessity of occurrence.
C) Denying the reality of human acts and affirming compulsion.
D) Affirming that God's will is eternal and that it necessitates.

Key Takeaway
The argument from divine will is refuted by denying the exhaustive division and the necessity of occurrence, preserving human choice in the Ash'arite framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your actions were predetermined, making you passive about your choices? How does this refutation challenge that feeling and call you to take responsibility even when you feel things are 'written'?
?
If God's will does not necessitate your actions, what does that imply about your accountability before Him? How should that change the way you approach your daily decisions, knowing He has not forced them?
?
The argument is refuted by denying a false dichotomy. In your own life, are there situations where you think you only have two options, when God's will may offer a third path? Name one such false dichotomy you face and how you might break it.

The Fifth Proof: Conflict of Wills
Presenting the fifth proof argument from conflicting wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hypothesis of conflicting wills between the servant and God leads to an absurdity, proving that the servant cannot be independent in his act.

Core Idea
If two independent wills conflict, a deadlock results—proving only one power can be truly independent.

This lesson presents the core of the fifth proof against the claim of the servant's independence. By examining a hypothetical scenario where a human's will opposes God's, we see that the only logical outcome is an impossibility. This forces us to accept that the servant's power is not independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Notice the two logical possibilities laid out in the hypothetical scenario of conflicting wills.
The proof is a conditional: if the servant were independent, then...
2
Understand the contradiction
Identify why both possibilities are impossible: either a direct contradiction or a preference without a preferrer.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the refutation
Recognize that the only way to avoid the absurdity is to deny the initial assumption of independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الخامس) الدليل الخامس لو كان فعله بقدرته، فإذا أراد تحريك جسم، وأراد الله سكونه . فإما أن يتفق المراد إن في الوقوع أو اللاوقوع وهو محال وإما أن يختلفا وهو ترجيح بلا مرجح، لأن التقدير استقلال القدرتين . وأجيب بأن التساوي في الاستقلال لا يمنع التفاوت في القوة فيقع مراد الله تعالى لكون قدرته أقوى . لو كان العبد مستقلاً بإيجاد فعله، فإذا فرضنا أنه أراد تحريك جسم في وقت)، وأراد الله تعالى سكونه في ذلك الوقت فإما أن يقع المرادان جميعاً وهو ظاهر الاستحالة قطعاً أو لا يقع شيء منهما وهو أيضاً محال لامتناع خلو الجسم في غير أن الحدوث عن الحركة والسكون، ولأن التخلف عن المقتضى لا يكون إلا لمانع ، ولا مانع لكل من المرادين سوى وقوع الآخر ، فلو امتنعا جميعاً لزم أن يقعا جميعاً وهو ظاهر الاستحالة،
He said (the fifth): The fifth proof: If his act were by his power, then if he wants to move a body and God wants its stillness, then either the two objects of will agree in occurrence or non-occurrence, which is impossible, or they differ, which is preferring without a preferrer, because it is assumed that the two powers are independent. And it was answered: Equality in independence does not prevent disparity in strength, so the object of God's will occurs because His power is stronger. If the servant were independent in bringing his act into existence, then if we suppose that he wants to move a body at a time, and God, exalted, wants its stillness at that time, then either both objects of will occur, which is clearly impossible; or neither of them occurs, which is also impossible because the body cannot be devoid of motion and stillness at the moment of coming into existence, and because the failure of an effect to follow its cause can only be due to an obstacle, and there is no obstacle for each of the two objects of will except the occurrence of the other. So if both are impossible, it would necessitate that both occur, which is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-dalīl al-khāmis) against the claim that the servant is independent in his act. The argument is a hypothetical conflict of wills: suppose a human wants to move a body (e.g., his hand) and God wants it to be still at that moment. There are two possibilities: either both objects of will agree (both occur or both do not occur) – but this is impossible because God's will and the servant's will are opposite; or they differ (one occurs, the other does not) – this would require a preferrer (murajjiḥ) without one, because both powers are assumed to be independent and equal. Therefore, the premise that the servant is independent must be false. The answer (jāba) is given: equality in independence does not deny disparity in strength (quwwa). God's power is infinitely stronger, so His will prevails. The passage then elaborates: if the servant were truly independent, a scenario of conflicting wills would lead to absurdity – either both occur (impossible) or neither occurs (also impossible, because a body cannot be devoid of motion and stillness at its moment of origination). If neither occurs, that would require both to occur as a consequence, which is a contradiction. Hence, the only coherent position is that God's power overrides, and the servant is not independent.

Key Terms
استقلال
قدرة
مرجح
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant can be fully independent in his acts alongside God's omnipotence.
Correction: The proof shows that independence of two agents with contradictory wills leads to a logical impossibility, so the servant cannot be truly independent.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities presented in the proof?
A) Both wills agree or differ.
B) Both occur or both do not occur.
C) One occurs or neither occurs.
D) The servant's will prevails or God's will prevails.

Key Takeaway
If the servant's power were independent, conflicting divine and human wills would lead to a rational absurdity, disproving independence for the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the moments when you insist on your own plans despite knowing that Allah has decreed otherwise, are you not unconsciously assuming that your will is independent? What would it mean today to genuinely let go of that assumption?
?
The proof shows that any conflict between your will and Allah's will must be resolved by Allah's preference—there is no room for a standoff. Is there an area in your life where you are still trying to assert your independence against Allah's decree? How will you surrender that area to Him?
?
If your power is not independent, then your successes and failures are ultimately from Allah. How does this reshape your pride in achievements or your despair over failures? What can you do today to practice tawakkul (reliance) over self-reliance?

The Answer: Divine Power Prevails Over Servant's Will
Presenting the answer to the fifth proof and its further elaboration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer to the fifth proof resolves the conflict by affirming inequality in strength, and see the detailed absurdity that follows from denying this answer.

Core Idea
Equality in independence does not require equality in strength—God's power is infinitely stronger.

This lesson continues from the previous one. After the proof of conflicting wills, an answer is given: the independence of the servant does not mean his power is equal to God's. The passage then elaborates by showing that if the servant were truly independent, an even more absurd conclusion would follow—namely, that both wills would have to occur simultaneously. This reinforces the Asʿarī position that the servant is not independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
Note the distinction between independence (istiqlāl) and strength (quwwa). The answer says equality in independence doesn't prevent disparity in strength.
This is the direct response to the proof's objection.
2
Follow the elaboration
Understand the detailed hypothetical that assumes independence and derives a contradiction: both objects of will either occur or not, both leading to absurdity.
This is a further reductio to show the necessity of the answer.
3
Draw the conclusion
Recognize that without the answer, an inescapable contradiction arises, so the only coherent position is that God's power is stronger and the servant is not independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الخامس) الدليل الخامس لو كان فعله بقدرته، فإذا أراد تحريك جسم، وأراد الله سكونه . فإما أن يتفق المراد إن في الوقوع أو اللاوقوع وهو محال وإما أن يختلفا وهو ترجيح بلا مرجح، لأن التقدير استقلال القدرتين . وأجيب بأن التساوي في الاستقلال لا يمنع التفاوت في القوة فيقع مراد الله تعالى لكون قدرته أقوى . لو كان العبد مستقلاً بإيجاد فعله، فإذا فرضنا أنه أراد تحريك جسم في وقت)، وأراد الله تعالى سكونه في ذلك الوقت فإما أن يقع المرادان جميعاً وهو ظاهر الاستحالة قطعاً أو لا يقع شيء منهما وهو أيضاً محال لامتناع خلو الجسم في غير أن الحدوث عن الحركة والسكون، ولأن التخلف عن المقتضى لا يكون إلا لمانع ، ولا مانع لكل من المرادين سوى وقوع الآخر ، فلو امتنعا جميعاً لزم أن يقعا جميعاً وهو ظاهر الاستحالة،
He said (the fifth): The fifth proof: If his act were by his power, then if he wants to move a body and God wants its stillness, then either the two objects of will agree in occurrence or non-occurrence, which is impossible, or they differ, which is preferring without a preferrer, because it is assumed that the two powers are independent. And it was answered: Equality in independence does not prevent disparity in strength, so the object of God's will occurs because His power is stronger. If the servant were independent in bringing his act into existence, then if we suppose that he wants to move a body at a time, and God, exalted, wants its stillness at that time, then either both objects of will occur, which is clearly impossible; or neither of them occurs, which is also impossible because the body cannot be devoid of motion and stillness at the moment of coming into existence, and because the failure of an effect to follow its cause can only be due to an obstacle, and there is no obstacle for each of the two objects of will except the occurrence of the other. So if both are impossible, it would necessitate that both occur, which is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-dalīl al-khāmis) against the claim that the servant is independent in his act. The argument is a hypothetical conflict of wills: suppose a human wants to move a body (e.g., his hand) and God wants it to be still at that moment. There are two possibilities: either both objects of will agree (both occur or both do not occur) – but this is impossible because God's will and the servant's will are opposite; or they differ (one occurs, the other does not) – this would require a preferrer (murajjiḥ) without one, because both powers are assumed to be independent and equal. Therefore, the premise that the servant is independent must be false. The answer (jāba) is given: equality in independence does not deny disparity in strength (quwwa). God's power is infinitely stronger, so His will prevails. The passage then elaborates: if the servant were truly independent, a scenario of conflicting wills would lead to absurdity – either both occur (impossible) or neither occurs (also impossible, because a body cannot be devoid of motion and stillness at its moment of origination). If neither occurs, that would require both to occur as a consequence, which is a contradiction. Hence, the only coherent position is that God's power overrides, and the servant is not independent.

Key Terms

مانع
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the elaboration, what would happen if neither object of will occurs?
A) The body becomes motionless.
B) Both must occur, which is impossible.
C) The conflict is resolved naturally.
D) The servant's will is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The answer to the fifth proof shows that disparity in strength resolves the apparent conflict, and denying this leads to an inescapable logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your carefully laid plans collapse despite your best efforts, do you truly accept that God's will has prevailed because His power is stronger? Or is there a part of you that resents this reality? Write down one specific instance and ask Allah for sincere submission.
?
This lesson shows that without God's superior power, logical contradictions would arise. Are there areas of your life where you unconsciously act as if your will is independent, ignoring that every breath depends on His permission? What would change if you began each day acknowledging that your strength is nothing compared to His?
?
If you genuinely believe that God's power is infinitely stronger than yours, why do you still worry about outcomes that are beyond your control? Ponder the Arabic phrase 'قدرته أقوى' (His power is stronger) and let it pierce your heart. Commit to reciting it whenever anxiety about the future creeps in.
